[HEADLINE] :  state begins accepting applications for expanded drug program
[TEXT]
albany,  n.y. (ap) - the enrollment period has arrived for a new
expansion  of new york's prescription drug program for senior
citizens  which advocates hope will ultimately double the number of
participants.
 income  eligibility levels will grow and copays and enrollment
fees  will drop for qualified participants starting jan. 1, 2001.
the  state will start to accept applications as of monday, oct. 2,
under  the bill approved by the state legislature and gov. george
pataki  earlier this year expanding the ''epic'' drug program.
currently,  about 124,000 new yorkers age 65 and older are in
epic,  according to the state health department. advocates say that
number  should double by 2003 under the more liberal qualifying
criteria.
 the  health department estimates that so far, seniors have filled
more  than 35 million prescriptions at lower epic costs and saved
more  than $825 million.
the  expansion of the program is expected to cost the state about
$180  million a year by 2003.
the  health department distributed about 10,000 newsletters to
agencies  serving the elderly since this summer summarizing the
changes  in the epic program. all the seniors already enrolled in
epic  were also notified of the impending changes, as were the 9,300
seniors  who were denied benefits or who canceled their
participation  in the program in 2000.
the  health department also began running newspaper
advertisements  about epic's expansion last week and plans to make
brochures  available to every pharmacy in the state starting this
week.  there are also television ads about the epic expansion in the
works  to start airing in early 2001.
information  is also available over the epic toll-free
information  line, 1-800-332-3742, and the health department's web
site  at www.health.state.ny.us.
''we  believe this will be an effective approach to not only get
the  word out but to increase enrollment in the program,'' said rob
kenny,  a spokesman for the state health department.
the  new york aarp is also funding radio ads across the state
about  the epic changes as well as distributing information to
seniors  through its local chapters.
the  aarp's legislative representative in albany, bill ferris,
said  his group hopes the promotional campaign will enroll the newly
eligible  as well as the currently eligible seniors who have never
signed  up.
''we  are not near the number of people who are eligible,'' he
said.  ''so if the outreach is effective, we are hoping to see
substantial  numbers.''
it  is unclear how many new yorkers have not enrolled in epic who
can.  but ferris noted that of the 2.4 million new yorkers age 65 or
older,  12 percent or about 300,000 have incomes below the federal
poverty  level. they would all qualify under the current rules.
seniors  are fed up with high prescription drug prices and should
take  advantage of the state's program if they can, said new york
aarp  manager of advocacy fred griesback.
''when  we are talking to our members, they are absolutely
appalled  by what they are paying for drugs,'' he said. ''it is
desperation  that people feel. it's like pouring money down the
sink.''
 [DOCNO] :  apw20000930.0081
[HEADLINE] :  gov. raps clinton-gore energy policy
[TEXT]
charleston,  w.va. (ap) -- west virginia gov. cecil underwood
criticized  the clinton-gore administration saturday for failing to
develop  a national energy policy.
the  administration needs to promote energy production from u.s.
sources  but instead ''treats american energy producers as the
enemy,''  underwood said in the republican party's weekly national
radio  address.
underwood's  remarks followed george w. bush's criticism of the
clinton  administration for putting ''our economy and the way of
life  it supports'' at risk by failing to develop a comprehensive
energy  policy.
bush  introduced a 10-year, $7.1 billion program to increase the
nation's  fuel sources.
underwood  criticized president clinton's recent decision to tap
the  nation's oil reserves because of high international prices.
the  decision was ''an ill-advised, election-year ploy to deplete
energy  reserves set aside for times of real crisis,'' underwood
said.  ''we would not be in this position if the clinton-gore team
had  done anything in the past eight years to work with energy
producers  in the united states instead of working against them.''
underwood,  who is involved in a tight re-election campaign
against  u.s. rep. bob wise, d-w.va., is a harsh critic of vice
president  al gore's push for an international global warming
treaty,  which calls for emissions cutbacks that would severely
tighten  regulatory control of coal-fired power plants.
''the  vice president's stated goals would devastate coal mining
in  my state and several states in appalachia and the mountains of
the  west,'' underwood said.
''coal  fired-power plants generate a majority of the nation's
electric  power, yet the gore approach would replace them -- i guess
with  windmills.''

[HEADLINE] :  singapore schools close after deaths
[TEXT]
singapore  (ap) -- singapore authorities on saturday ordered all
kindergartens,  child-care centers and nursery schools to close
temporarily  after three children died, possibly from a viral
infection.
 a  14-month-old girl and her 2-year-old brother died saturday,
and  authorities suspect the cause was hand, foot and mouth disease,
a  government statement said. a 2-year-boy also is believed to have
died  from the illness earlier this month.
a  government statement advised parents to ''keep their children
away  from crowded places,'' and to consult a doctor if their
children  showed such symptoms as fever, sore throat and sometimes
blistering  rashes on the hands and feet.
the  closures will be in effect for at least seven to 10 days,
pending  a review by officials, singapore environment ministry
spokeswoman  michelle tan said.
they  will affect more than 37,500 children in 557 child care
centers  and some 103,000 children in more than 400 kindergartens,
she  said.
tan  said that 363 cases of hand, foot and mouth disease have
been  reported in the wealthy southeast asian city-state of 3.2
million  people since mid-september.
the  disease is not usually life-threatening but can sometimes
involve  potentially fatal complications such as meningitis,
encephalitis  and inflammation of the heart muscles.
more  than 50 children died in taiwan during an outbreak of hand,
foot  and mouth disease in 1998. thirty died of the illness in the
malaysian  state of sarawak on borneo island in 1997.

[HEADLINE] :  hmo lawsuit bill tops legislative agenda
[TEXT]
trenton,  n.j. (ap) - legislation to enable patients to sue their
health  insurance carriers for malpractice and another bill
appropriating  $150 million for transportation projects top the
state  senate's voting session agenda for monday.
the  right-to-sue bill would allow patients to take their hmos to
court  for negligence if they can show the denial or delay of care
adversely  affected their health.
''hmos  must be held accountable for the quality health care that
new  jerseyans need and deserve, said jerry flanagan, health care
advocate  for new jersey public interest research group, which has
backed  the bill. opponents warn it will invite a flood of frivolous
lawsuits.
 the  $150 million for transportation projects represents the
second  decrease from a $500 million transportation bond issue
approved  by the voters in 1999. half the bond issue was dedicated
to  counties and municipalities for bridge repair and replacement.
the  other half goes to a variety of state transportation projects,
including  roads, bridges and bikeways.
under  the bill to be considered monday, $25 million would go to
counties  and municipalities for bridge repairs and replacements and
$125  million would go to state transportation projects. in the
previous  decrease earlier this year, counties and municipalities
got  $125 million while $80 million went to state projects.
another  bill on the senate list would appropriate $3.25 million
to  cranford to help finance the first phase of a four-phase plan of
flood  control projects. cranford was hit by severe flooding from
the  rahway river during hurricane floyd last year, suffering $15
million  in damage. it also experienced flooding in 1992, 1996 and
1997.
 volunteer  ambulance squads and fire companies might get some
protection  from overregulation under a bill that would require an
impact  statement for any proposed regulation affecting them. the
intent  is to keep from driving volunteer first aid squads and fire
companies  out of business.
instruction  on compulsive gambling would be included as part of
the  required curriculum in health and physical education under
another  senate bill scheduled for debate.
another  bill is aimed at ensuring that agreements made and
contracts  signed in cyberspace hold the same legal weight as paper
transactions.
 in  addition to considering 29 bills, the senate will hear from
former  new york democratic rep. shirley a. chisholm. chisholm, the
first  holder of the wynona m. lipman chair in women's political
leadership  at the eagleton institute of rutgers university, will
address  the senate session when it opens at 2 p.m. on monday.
the  assembly meets thursday.

[HEADLINE] :  restored fire towers offer great views and a glimpse of history
[TEXT]
hunter,  n.y. (ap) - from the top of hunter mountain, there's a
panoramic  view of the black head range to the north, the indian
head  wilderness to the east, and the spruceton valley to the west.
such  grand, 360-degree vistas are rare in the catskill
mountains,  where trees block at least part of the view from most of
the  rounded summits. but on hunter, a fire tower rising above the
balsams  lets hikers expand their horizons - if they're brave enough
to  climb 80 steep steps to a wind-buffeted, precarious perch.
the  steel structure, gleaming in the sun with a fresh coat of
silver  paint, is one of four towers recently restored to safe
climbing  condition in the catskills. restoration work is almost
finished  on a fifth.
in  the adirondacks, three renovated towers are open to the
public  and work is under way to repair several others.
restoring  the towers is a labor of love for a diverse group of
people,  most of them volunteers. they include hikers, history
buffs,  conservationists, tourism boosters, local governments, and
nonprofit  groups seeking to preserve the heritage of the mountain
regions.
 ''the  network of fire spotters had a lot to do with protecting
the  forest preserve,'' said steven engelhart, executive director of
adirondack  architectural heritage. ''it's an interesting chapter in
adirondack  history.''
''there's  a romantic appeal to fire towers,'' engelhart said.
''people  are drawn to them, much as they are drawn to
lighthouses.''
 the  state forest commissioner proposed the construction of fire
towers  throughout the catskills and adirondacks in 1909. a wooden
tower  on hunter mountain, later replaced by a steel structure, was
the  first of 13 towers built that year.
it  was an era when sparks from coal-fired trains ignited the
dead  timber left by clear-cutting loggers, causing catastrophic
forest  fires. using hand-cranked telephones, spotters were able to
relay  an early warning before the fires raged out of control.
with  the advent of aerial observation, the reforestation of
logged-over  land, and better fire prevention and control, the
towers  eventually became obsolete. state officials started phasing
them  out in the 1980s. the last fire observer retired in 1990.
many  of the towers were dismantled. others, in rickety
condition,  were closed to the public for safety reasons.
five  steel towers remain in the 300,000-acre catskill park. in
1997,  a group of volunteers formed the catskill fire tower
restoration  project to raise money and recruit workers to repair
the  towers and open them to hikers.
the  project is cosponsored by the state department of
environmental  conservation and the catskill center for conservation
and  development. local committees coordinate work parties and raise
money  through community events such as quilt raffles and square
dances,  as well as the sale of patches, t-shirts and postcards.
some  of the cabins where fire observers lived near the base of
towers  are also being restored, with plans to open them as living
museums.
 at  the hadley tower, near the southeastern edge of the
adirondack  park, and blue mountain, in the center of the
adirondacks,  summit stewards sometimes work on weekends to share
fire  tower lore and help visitors identify the peaks and lakes in
the  distance. there has also been a summit steward at the mount
arab  tower in the northern adirondacks.
leaflets  with historical and natural history information are
available  at the trailheads to hadley, blue, goodnow, and
poke-o-moonshine  mountains in the adirondacks.
''all  these preservation efforts aspire to have an interpreter
in  the tower during the summer at least,'' engelhart said.
''besides  coordinating firefighting activities, the fire observers
were  early conservation educators. they told hikers about plants,
wildlife,  what they were seeing in the landscape. interpreters can
serve  this role again.''
the  red hill fire tower is the only one in the catskills with a
formal  interpretive program, with volunteers staffing the tower and
restored  observer's cabin every weekend through october, said helen
budrock  of the catskill center.
''eventually  we hope to have interpreters at all the towers,''
budrock  said. the catskill center also is working on a web page
about  the towers, which will be accessible through the center's web
site  (www.catskillcenter.org).
in  addition to the hunter mountain tower, restored catskill fire
towers  are also open to the public on balsam lake mountain in
hardenburgh,  red hill in denning, and overlook mountain in
woodstock.  the tremper mountain tower in phoenicia is expected to
be  open by early november.
hiking  to the towers isn't terribly strenuous, since most of
them  have old jeep trails that were once used by fire observers but
now  are closed to motor vehicles.
to  get to the hunter tower, you can take a scenic chairlift ride
from  the hunter mountain ski center and then hike up two miles of
rocky  trail and old road. there are also several other trails up
the  mountain.
a  new guide book called ''views from on high: fire tower trails
of  the adirondacks and catskills'' is being published by the
adirondack  mountain club this fall.
---
 on  the net:
catskill  center: www.catskillcenter.org
adirondack  mountain club: www.adk.org

[HEADLINE] :  facts about the impending expansion of the epic drug program
[TEXT]
-higher  benefits and lower payments go into effect on jan. 1,
2001.  the sign-up period for the expanded program begins monday.
-people  age 65 and older are eligible.
-under  the new rules, individuals making $35,000 a year (up from
the  current $18,500 a year) or married couples making $50,000 a
year  (up from the current $24,400 a year) will become eligible.
-seniors  in the expanded income brackets can join a new
deductible  plan under which they can buy prescription drugs for
copays  after spending about 3 percent of their incomes on
prescription  drugs.
-the  copay schedule will change. current copays are $3 for
prescription  drugs costing up to $8; $5 for drugs costing $8.01 to
$13;  $7 for drugs costing $13.01 to $23; $10 for drugs costing
$23.01  to $33, and $23 for drugs costing more than $33.
the  new copay schedule will be: a $3 copay for prescriptions
costing  up to $15; $7 for prescriptions costing $15.01 to $35; $15
for  drugs costing $35.01 to $55, and $20 for drugs costing more
than  $55.
-a  fee plan will be available to seniors in the lowest income
levels  - up to $20,000 for individuals and up to $26,000 for
married  couples. the fees range from $2 to $75 per quarter.
-the  current rule against caps or limits on benefits will
continue  under the expanded program. so will the rule under which
seniors  do not have to continue paying copays if they reach a
certain  limit in a year, based on a seniors' income.
-all  prescription medicines are covered under the program, as
well  as insulin and syringes. over-the-counter medications are not.
source:  new york state department of health.

[HEADLINE] :  india's pm expands cabinet
[TEXT]
new  delhi, india (ap) -- prime minister atal bihari vajpayee
added  six members to his cabinet including one of india's most
popular  female politicians -- just before two other ministers
resigned  to protest a hike in oil prices.
mamata  banerjee, the federal minister for railways, announced
her  resignation in the eastern city of calcutta along with her
trinamul  congress party colleague ajit kumar panja, the junior
foreign  affairs minister, united news of india reported.
banerjee  did not withdraw support from the government -- her
party  is a partner in vajpayee's ruling coalition -- and the
decision  not does immediately threaten the administration.
banerjee  was protesting the government's decision friday to
increase  prices of subsidized diesel, gasoline, kerosene and other
oil  products to make up for lost revenue from the recent rise in
global  oil prices.
if  the two resignations are accepted by ceremonial president
kocheril  raman narayanan, the cabinet would have 73 members.
narayanan  would act on the prime minister's advice.
shortly  before banerjee announced her decision, narayanan
administered  the oath of office in new delhi to sushma swaraj, a
former  federal minister, m. venkaiah naidu, a former spokesman of
vajpayee's  bharatiya janata party, and four other first-time
ministers.
 their  responsibilities were not immediately announced.
swaraj,  48, has for years been one of india's most well-known
female  politicians, and has often been assigned tough tasks by her
party,  the bharatiya janata party, in the past.
in  1999, she was asked to become the chief minister, or top
elected  official, of the delhi state government weeks before
elections  to help the fortunes of the unpopular government. months
later,  she was chosen to run against sonia gandhi, leader of the
main  opposition congress party, in a tough election battle that
swaraj  lost.
late  friday, the gasoline prices were increased by six cents per
liter.  that raises the cost of the fuel used for most cars,
scooters  and three-wheel vehicles 65 cents per liter.
diesel  and kerosene, which is used for cooking, were also hiked
six  cents per liter, while low-pressure gas, used for cooking and
heating,  was raised by 87 cents per liter.

[HEADLINE] :  8 palestinians killed in clashes
[TEXT]
jerusalem  (ap) -- thousands of palestinian rock throwers and
several  gunmen clashed with israeli troops saturday in what
palestinians  said was a religious war over a bitterly contested
jerusalem  shrine. nine palestinians, including two teen-agers, were
killed  and 325 demonstrators injured.
it  was the second day of deadly clashes in what has become the
worst  violence in the west bank and gaza strip in four years. on
friday,  six palestinians were killed and close to 200 were injured.
''the  battle for jerusalem has begun,'' said bassem naim, a
palestinian  activist.
palestinian  gunmen and israeli troops exchanged fire in two
areas,  near the jewish settlement of netzarim in gaza and south of
the  west bank town of nablus.
at  the netzarim junction, a palestinian fired shots at an
israeli  army outpost from behind a red truck. soldiers returned
fire,  and several palestinians took cover on the ground, covering
their  heads with their hands, their faces pressed on the asphalt.
one  palestinian man screamed in pain when he was shot in the knee.
paramedics  ran into the line of fire to drag him to safety.
shifa  hospital in gaza city reported three dead -- a 12-year-old
boy,  a palestinian policeman and an ambulance driver. a fourth
palestinian,  from the town of khan yunis, was comatose with a
bullet  wound to the head.
in  the west bank, six palestinians were killed by israeli fire,
including  five who died in a gun battle between a dozen masked
gunmen  and israeli troops near nablus. the gunmen crouched behind
walls  and olive trees as they aimed at the israelis who took cover
behind  jeeps.
''the  israelis must understand that palestinians can also shoot
and  kill,'' said a 25-year-old gunman armed with an m-16 assault
rifle  who would not give his name.
hospital  officials initially said one of those killed in nablus
was  a 14-year-old boy. however, the victim's parents gave his age
as  16.
violence  erupted across the west bank, gaza and arab
neighborhoods  of jerusalem. huge plumes of black smoke from burning
tires  rose high above palestinian towns, and streets were littered
with  rocks and overturned garbage bins.
in  hebron, rioters running out of rocks had them ferried to the
scene  in taxis. helmeted israeli soldiers fired rubber-coated steel
pellets  and live rounds from behind jeeps and walls.
palestinians  say the trigger for the violence was a visit
thursday  by israel's hawkish opposition leader, ariel sharon, to
the  disputed jerusalem hilltop known to jews as the temple mount
and  to muslims as haram as-sharif, or noble sanctuary.
in  clashes at the compound friday, six palestinians were killed
and  close to 200 wounded. ''we're going to teach them not to touch
our  holy place,'' said khalil natash, 21, a stone-thrower in the
west  bank town of hebron, who carried a black flag of mourning for
those  killed. ''we will burn the ground underneath the israelis to
stop  them from touching our holy place.''
israeli  prime minister ehud barak appealed to palestinian leader
yasser  arafat to restore calm. he said israel has been showing
''maximum  restraint,'' but would do what was necessary to protect
its  citizens and restore order. the violence erupted as israelis
marked  the jewish new year.
barak's  senior policy adviser, danny yatom, said he believed
there  was still a possibility to resume peace negotiations once the
violence  has died down. ''i really hope the peace process won't be
hurt,  but i can't be sure,'' yatom said.
israeli-palestinian  peace talks are hung up because of rival
sovereignty  claims to the jerusalem shrine. neither side wants the
other  to have full control. all u.s. compromise proposals have been
rejected  so far by the negotiators.
palestinian  peace negotiator nabil shaath said israeli security
forces  committed ''premeditated murder'' at the jerusalem shrine
friday.  the violence underscored palestinian demands that israel
must  withdraw from east jerusalem, the sector the palestinians
claim  as a future capital, shaath said.
marwan  barghouti, the fatah leader in the west bank, said he
expected  confrontations to intensify in the coming days.
''the  way the israelis are behaving now in the palestinian
territories  and israel's reluctance to express regret over what has
been  done at the mosque is encouraging the spread of the
confrontations,''  he said.
many  israelis felt the palestinians were responsible. ''despite
the  fact that there is a peace process, they (the palestinians)
want  to ruin everything,'' said meirav buchbut, 21, a bookkeeper
from  the town of netanya. ''there won't be peace. there will only
be  war.''
across  the west bank, gaza strip and east jerusalem,
palestinians  observed a general strike and day of mourning
saturday.  shops were closed and children were sent home from
school.  israel barred its citizens from entering the
palestinian-controlled  areas to avoid further friction.

[HEADLINE] :  franks, corzine differ on federal government's role in public education
[TEXT]
trenton,  n.j. (ap) - in some ways, u.s. senate candidates jon
corzine  and bob franks agree on what and how children should be
taught.
 democrat  corzine and republican franks part company, however,
over  the federal government's role in public education.
both  corzine, the millionaire wall street retiree, and franks,
the  union county congressman, support ''character'' education to
promote  values in children and after-school programs to provide
them  with a safe haven, for example.
after  that, differences emerge.
corzine  has also made college scholarships a key feature of a
campaign  platform driven by television advertising, although he
recently  tempered his promises somewhat with caveats to control
runaway  costs. some took it as a signal he may shift away from the
universal  promises offered during the primary to a more measured
set  of proposals for the general election.
as  for the public schools, both candidates have laid out a
variety  of proposals on what polls suggests is a hot-button issue.
franks  believes the federal government ought to hand over more
control  to local school districts and parents.
conversely,  corzine proposes that the federal government
increase  its involvement by targeting increased funding to solve
specific  flaws in public education.
corzine  ''is not only committed to ensuring that the federal
government  plays a bigger role, but will target the funds to the
public  education system's biggest needs,'' said campaign
spokeswoman  ivette mendez.
in  new jersey, the federal government contributed 3.7 percent of
education  costs in the 1998-99 school year, the last year for which
statistics  are available, according to the new jersey school boards
association.
 among  corzine's various calls for more federal investment is a
$50  billion program over 10 years to ensure that preschool programs
are  accredited, meet health and safety standards, and supply
well-trained  and compensated staff.
overall,  corzine wants to see federal education aid increase to
enable  local districts to hire and train more certified teachers,
reduce  class sizes, enhance school safety and stem substance abuse,
mendez  said.
the  democrat supports interest-free school construction bonds
with  tax credits to help state and local governments modernize,
repair  and build public schools, mendez said.
unlike  corzine, franks wants to decentralize the federal
government's  hold on public education.
''bob  franks believes parents, teachers and administrators know
what's  best for our children. they should be the ones empowered to
make  decisions regarding our children's education, not a washington
bureaucracy,''  said campaign spokesman peter demarco.
franks  supports federal block grants to give local officials the
flexibility  to meet local needs. in return, the districts would
have  to meet higher education accountability standards, demarco
said.
 franks'  decentralized approach irks the corzine campaign, which
says  there aren't enough accountability strings attached to franks'
favored  form of aid.
corzine  also takes franks to task for voting in 1995 to
dismantle  the federal department of education, but franks' campaign
said  the resolution was merely advisory and never implemented.
''what  they cited was not binding language,'' said demarco. ''it
only  called on appropriators to consider eliminating the department
of  education.'' demarco noted the provision was a small part of a
300-page  report.
''the  goal of the report was not to reduce the financial
commitment  to education, but to have more federal money find its
way  to classrooms,'' he said.
franks  said he will press to triple federal funding for
educating  the disabled and handicapped, and he has proposed a $1
billion  loan program to help charter schools pay for new
facilities.
 franks  also voted for a bill that would allow states to
distribute  federal funds to parents who need help paying private
school  tuition. corzine opposes vouchers for private school costs.

[HEADLINE] :  clinton tries to energize black voters in absence of anti-giuliani base
[TEXT]
albany,  n.y. (ap) - hillary rodham clinton, trying to stitch
together  a coalition that will carry her to the u.s. senate, faces
a  stiffer test in making sure blacks go the polls since new york
city  mayor rudolph giuliani left the race.
her  success at mobilizing the black vote could determine the
outcome  of a close race against her new republican rival, rick
lazio,  a congressman from long island who has not generated the
sort  of animosity among blacks that the mayor did.
''with  giuliani in the race, there would have been an
avalanche''  of black votes for clinton, said state comptroller h.
carl  mccall, the only black ever elected to statewide office in new
york.
 ''the  difference is, we'll need a bigger effort to energize the
community,  but it will be energized on the basis of the issues that
hillary  clinton has been talking about,'' said mccall, a democrat
considering  a run for governor in 2002.
some  black leaders insist she must do more.
''we  all knew why we didn't want giuliani in the senate,'' said
the  rev. al sharpton, a prominent new york city activist. ''with
giuliani  out, you've got to give us a reason to put you in the
senate,  and i'm not sure that has been done.''
giuliani's  critics say the mayor lacks sympathy toward new york
city's  large minority population and has been too supportive of a
police  force that many blacks view as overly aggressive.
but  polls show that lazio is doing no better among blacks than
giuliani.  a sept. 21 poll from marist college's institute for
public  opinion found clinton supported by 91 percent of black
voters.  a marist poll in march, when giuliani was in the race, had
her  support at 87 percent among blacks.
the  mayor left the race in mid-may after learning he had
prostate  cancer. what the polls don't reflect is whether his
withdrawal  might reduce black turnout.
''there  was little doubt the polarizing effect of giuliani would
have  put turnout of minorities off the charts,'' said marist
pollster  lee miringoff.
to  energize black voters, the clinton campaign has organized
visits  by rev. jesse jackson and san francisco mayor willie brown.
the  first lady has been visiting black churches - and winning
praise  from parishioners.
the  clintons ''were brought up the way we were brought up - to
treat  everyone fairly,'' said carol wilkins of queens, who works in
a  housing project. ''i hope she makes it.''
''she'll  really work hard for us,'' said elsie murray of
brooklyn,  who works for an insurance company. ''she loves people,
she's  sincere, she's not a phony.''
clinton  has stepped up interviews with media outlets geared
toward  black and hispanic communities.
she  and president clinton also have courted black leaders from
new  york with overnight stays at the white house. mccall and his
wife,  former new york city deputy mayor joyce brown, stayed in the
lincoln  bedroom. the rev. floyd flake, a former congressman, and
manhattan  borough president c. virginia fields also slept over.
clinton  aides are counting on the natural draw of a presidential
election,  plus phone banks and street volunteers, to help offset
any  falloff in black interest because of giuliani's withdrawal.
mccall  said there are plans to have president clinton speak to
''targeted  groups'' in the state just before the election.
''the  bottom line is that people in communities of color relate
to  president clinton and if he is in the state, actively supporting
hillary,  they will duly note that and it will duly add to their
enthusiasm  for hillary,'' said clinton campaign manager bill de
blasio.
 de  blasio says a get-out-the-vote effort in minority communities
in  1998 was instrumental in helping elect democrats in new york.
but  data shows blacks tend to register and vote at a lower rate
than  whites. while about 17 percent of new york's voting age
population  is black, exit polling found that just 9 percent of
voters  in 1998 were black.
to  energize black voters, sharpton said, clinton must speak out
more  forcefully on ''issues that resonate in our community,'' such
as  racial profiling, police brutality and education.
''you  cannot be vague and nebulous and then expect a vote that
is  specific,'' he said.

[HEADLINE] :  us, eu to delay trade tax sanctions
[TEXT]
washington  (ap) -- the united states and the european union
reached  agreement saturday to delay imposition of a possible $4
billion  in economic sanctions against the united states in a trade
dispute  involving tax breaks for american exports.
the  deal was announced by the clinton administration, which had
been  negotiating against a midnight saturday deadline for
overhauling  u.s. tax laws to come into compliance with the adverse
ruling  from the world trade organization.
under  the procedural agreement, congress will have until nov. 1
to  pass the legislation. the eu agreed not to impose any penalties
until  a separate wto panel can rule on whether the new legislation
complies  with wto rules.
''the  united states and european union today demonstrated a
commitment  to avoid escalating trans-atlantic trade tensions and
managing  this wto trade dispute responsibly,'' u.s. trade
representative  charlene barshefsky said in a statement.
the  european commission, in a statement, said the eu ''prefers
to  avoid escalation of the dispute at this stage.''
the  dispute involves the biggest case the united states has lost
before  the geneva-based wto, which ruled that america's system of
granting  tax breaks to u.s. companies that send goods abroad did
not  comply with wto rules.
congress  faced a sunday deadline from the wto for passing new
legislation.  when it became clear that the deadline would not be
met,  the administration began negotiations with the eu in an effort
to  postpone any threatened sanctions.
deputy  treasury secretary stuart eizenstat stressed that it was
critical  that congress pass legislation before it adjourns for the
fall  campaign.
''we  cannot emphasize strongly enough how critical it is that
congress  complete action on the (foreign sales corporation) repeal
and  replacement legislation as expeditiously as possible,''
eizenstat  said in a statement.
created  to offset an eu tax rebate for its exporters, the tax
program  permits u.s. companies to reduce income taxes by 15 percent
through  export subsidiaries set up in offshore tax havens such as
barbados  and the virgin islands. microsoft, boeing and general
motors  are only a few of the 6,000 companies, with 4.8 million
workers,  that get the $4.1 billion annual tax break.
saturday's  agreement with the 15-nation eu does not involve the
substance  of the dispute; rather, it establishes the procedural
guidelines  by which both sides will abide.
it  is important, however, because it will protect the united
states  from eu trade sanctions while a wto panel rules on the
replacement  legislation sought from congress. the deal the two
sides  reached was modeled after procedures used by canada and
australia  in a wto dispute involving salmon.
the  eu contends that the changes in the export tax law under
consideration  by congress do not go far enough to meet the wto's
objections  that the tax breaks amount to an illegal trade subsidy.
despite  white house pressure -- and an overwhelming favorable
vote  in the house on the legislation -- senate democrats blocked
attempts  this week by republican leaders to bring the bill up for
debate.  the dispute centers on republican efforts to limit
amendments  and democrats' insistence on their rights to offer them.
in  some cases, senators want to change the legislation, which
would  set up a replacement system of trade tax breaks on foreign
sales  for about 6,000 u.s. corporations. the new tax breaks would
come  to $4.5 billion over 10 years.
sen.  richard bryan, d-nev., is pushing an amendment that would
deny  the tax benefit to pharmaceutical companies that sell drugs in
the  united states at vastly higher prices than overseas.
other  senators want to deny tobacco companies the benefits,
arguing  that the government should not provide tax breaks while
waging  a war against smoking. a similar effort failed in the house.
still  other lawmakers want to attach completely unrelated
measures.  sen. russell feingold, d-wis., sought a vote on a
campaign  finance reform amendment on the trade tax bill, while sen.
edward  m. kennedy, d-mass., has threatened try to attach language
raising  the $5.15-an-hour minimum wage on several pieces of
legislation.
 senate  majority leader trent lott, r-miss., expressed
frustration  at the blocking tactics after he tried to bring the tax
measure  to the floor thursday. he expressed hope that the senate
would  be able to pass the proposal ''in the next few days.''
------

[HEADLINE] :  search protection leads court agenda
[TEXT]
washington  (ap) -- difficult questions about americans'
constitutional  protection against unreasonable searches -- while in
their  homes, cars and even in hospital beds -- lead the supreme
court's  agenda as the justices begin their 2000-2001 term monday.
some  observers, however, say the most important day for the
nation's  highest court will be election day. the next president
will  choose the next members of a court that has been divided 5-4
on  some of the nation's most explosive issues.
the  court this term also is confronting a major challenge to the
nation's  premier environmental law, the clean air act, and will
decide  whether people can sue states to enforce the federal ban on
discrimination  against the disabled.
the  term that ended in june was a blockbuster. the justices
upheld  the miranda warnings police must give before questioning
criminal  suspects, let the boy scouts ban homosexual troop leaders
and  struck down a state's ''partial-birth'' abortion law.
they  also banned group prayers at public high school football
games  and prevented rape victims from suing their attackers in
federal  courts.
the  court last week cleared its plate of the massive microsoft
antitrust  dispute by sending it to a federal appeals court,
delaying  a final ruling perhaps for years.
the  47 cases granted review for the new term -- with about two
dozen  more to be added in coming months -- lack the political punch
of  last year's cases but still will touch the lives of many
ordinary  americans.
''it's  already looming as a big year for the fourth amendment,''
which  protects americans against unreasonable searches and arrests,
said  steven shapiro of the american civil liberties union.
a  case that could affect the nation's 185 million licensed
drivers  asks whether police can arrest people for traffic
violations  punishable only by a fine. a texas woman, whose lawyers
call  her a ''typical motorist,'' says police went too far when they
arrested,  handcuffed and jailed her because she and her children
were  not wearing seatbelts in the family pickup truck.
other  cases illustrate ''the pressure that the war on drugs has
exerted  on the fourth amendment in particular and the constitution
in  general,'' shapiro said.
the  justices will decide whether police can set up traffic
checkpoints  and stop motorists in hopes of catching people who sell
or  use illegal drugs.
police  generally need a warrant or a reason to suspect someone
of  a crime before detaining them for several minutes, and a lower
court  ruled that drug checkpoints by indianapolis police likely
amounted  to unreasonable seizures. but the supreme court previously
has  upheld roadblocks aimed at catching drunken drivers.
another  war-on-drugs case asks whether public hospitals can test
pregnant  patients for drug use and tell police who tested positive.
a  south carolina hospital tested patients' urine without a court
warrant,  and if the result were positive, women were arrested for
endangering  the fetus.
a  group of women sued but a federal appeals court upheld the
tests,  saying hospitals had a ''substantial interest'' in seeking
to  reduce cocaine use by pregnant women.
a  case that was among a dozen granted review last week, when the
justices  got a head start on their new term, asks whether police
need  a search warrant to use a device to detect heat coming from
someone's  home. an oregon man was charged with growing marijuana
after  police said a heat-detecting device showed an unusual amount
of  heat coming from the roof over his garage.
still  another marijuana-search case involves an illinois man who
was  prevented by police from going inside his home alone until the
officers  could get a warrant to search for the drug.
the  clean air act dispute could yield a hugely important ruling
on  environmental protection that might also limit other federal
agencies'  authority to write rules to implement laws passed by
congress.
 industry  groups want the environmental protection agency to
consider  costs, in addition to health benefits, in setting federal
air-quality  standards. the case began as a dispute over the epa's
1997  standards for reducing smog and soot.
''the  argument is not that we want to turn back the clock'' on
environmental  protection, said edward warren, a lawyer for the u.s.
chamber  of commerce. instead, he said the issue is ''how are we
going  to do the most with what we have in solving environmental
problems?''
 but  frank o'donnell of the clean air trust called the
air-quality  standards ''the heart and lungs of the clean air act''
and  said a supreme court win for industry would mean ''more
pollution,  more health damage for all of us.''
the  justices might use the disability-bias case to forge ahead
with  what some legal scholars call a states' rights revolution -- a
series  of rulings that have sharply tipped the federal-state
balance  of governmental power toward the states. in january the
court  ruled that state employees cannot go into federal court to
sue  over age bias.
now,  the justices have agreed to decide whether state employees
can  sue in federal court to enforce the 1990 americans with
disabilities  act, a law that aims to protect the disabled against
job  bias. the law is perhaps best known for requiring wheelchair
ramps  in buildings across the country.
the  court's decision could sweep broadly enough to affect all
ada  lawsuits filed against state governments, not just those filed
by  state employees.
------

[HEADLINE] :  german unification was unstoppable
[TEXT]
london  (ap) -- german unification seemed unthinkable when the
berlin  wall unexpectedly opened on nov. 9, 1989. before christmas
it  began to look inevitable.
just  19 days after jubilant crowds first poured through
checkpoint  charlie, while his allies were urging caution in dealing
with  the rapidly unfolding drama, chancellor helmut kohl offered a
plan  to tie east germany and west germany into one nation. by dec.
9,  european union leaders reaffirmed germany's right to
unification.  ten months later it happened.
ten  years after german reunification, the berlin wall and the
country  called east germany have vanished, and europe is just as
stable  and peaceful as before.
but  at the time, there were fears in many capitals that
reunification  could disrupt the balance of power and provoke a
soviet  response, and there were long memories of two world wars.
what  couldn't be known at the time was how weak moscow was, and the
extent  to which chancellor helmut kohl had correctly judged the
hunger  for union in both parts of germany.
''before  1989 i can remember in our discussions nobody spoke
about  the possibility of germany reunification,'' wilfried martens,
prime  minister of belgium from 1979 to 1992, recalled in an
interview  with the associated press in brussels.
instantly,  the issue became pressing business.
martens  recalled that italian premier giulio andreotti expressed
some  doubts, but that the most vociferous opponent of german
unification  was british prime minister margaret thatcher. and
thatcher  found french president francois mitterrand ''still more
concerned  than i was.''
thatcher  thought she had an ally in mitterrand, and they had two
private  meetings during a european union summit in december.
''he  observed that in history the germans were a people in
constant  movement and flux. at this i produced from my handbag a
map  showing the various configurations of germany in the past,
which  were not altogether reassuring about the future. we talked
through  what precisely we might do,'' thatcher wrote in ''the
downing  street years,'' her 1993 memoirs.
mitterrand,  in her view, decided to do nothing, and that dashed
her  hopes of slowing kohl's rapid moves.
guy  cabanel, a conservative member of the french senate,
recalled  his own misgivings at the time.
''there  were two fears: the existential fear of germany of the
french  people and that the reunification would happen in disorder
and  that it would lead to unrest in what was then the ussr,
provoking  military troubles,'' said cabanel.
''i  was more leaning in favor of a harmoniously negotiated
reunification,  and of avoiding as much as possible the presence of
the  crowd,'' cabanel said. ''there was also the fear that germany
would  lose interest in european construction, to concentrate its
efforts  on reunification.''
thatcher,  then fighting moves to draw britain deeper into the
european  political and economic ambit, feared the reverse -- that to
gain  unification, kohl would accept tying germany into an
ever-closer  european union. the momentum toward a single germany
accelerated  in february 1990, when soviet leader mikhail gorbachev
signaled  his acceptance by saying, ''the unity of the german nation
must  be decided by the germans themselves.''
on  march 18, the newly formed alliance for germany, a
pro-unification  party, won 48 percent of the vote in east germany's
first  free election.
six  days later, thatcher met prominent academics at chequers,
her  official country residence, to ponder whether german
unification  was a good idea, and whether it could be stopped.
the  men she invited didn't share her fears.
''we  were extremely respectful towards present-day germany,''
oxford  university historian norman stone wrote years later.
thatcher  shocked some of the academics with her antagonism
toward  germany and germans.
''as  we were leaving i remarked to an english colleague that the
only  word she refrained from using in connection with germany was
'huns,'''  fritz stern of columbia university in new york later
recalled.
 by  that time, stern said, the die was cast.
''when  we met at chequers, the point had already been reached
where  east germany was bankrupt. the wall had been breached. it was
evident  that a majority in the gdr (german democratic republic)
favored  unification,'' stern said in an interview last year with
the  swiss newspaper neue zuercher zeitung.
''the  only ones who could have prevented it back then were the
russians,''  stern said.
swedish  journalist per landin, author of ''last tango in the
ddr,''  a 1991 book about the demise of east germany, was critical
of  reunification at the time.
''i  thought it went too fast,'' he said in an interview. ''it
wasn't  really a reunification as much as an annexation to or an
enlargement  of west germany.''
he  has changed his mind. ''it's been better than i had expected,
even  though it is an awesome mental change that will take several
generations''  to complete, he said.
belgium's  martens believes the doubters have been proved wrong.
in  the former east germany and east berlin, ''the changes are
incredible,  the investments were amazing in the infrastructure, the
modernization.  when we see berlin now it's not just a renovation,
it's  been a real revolution.''
''of  course there are still social and psychological problems.
the  integration is still going on. they have unified, but they
still  have to be integrated,'' martens said.
thatcher  concluded that she had been right to oppose rapid
unification,  but wrong to think she could do anything to stop it.
''most  observers were unaware of the nationalist passion for
german  unity that burned in the east,'' thatcher wrote.
''in  the event,'' thatcher said, ''the desire for unity among
germans  on both sides of the elbe proved irresistible.''
------
 editor's  note: robert barr was among the ap reporters who
covered  the fall of the berlin wall. paul ames in brussels, anne
pandolfi  in stockholm and christophe bernard in paris contributed
to  this report.

[HEADLINE] :  budget fight shows gop changes
[TEXT]
washington  (ap) -- this year's budget fight, with both parties
bidding  up federal spending to record levels, underlines the stark
change  in republican rhetoric and action since the newt
gingrich-led  gop began controlling congress in 1995.
the  new budget year that began sunday finds republican leaders
emphasizing  that they would use annual federal surpluses first to
reduce  the national debt, then to cut taxes and -- yes -- increase
spending  for schools and other priorities.
clearly,  they prefer less spending than president clinton wants.
even  so, with the white house and both chambers of congress at
stake  in next month's elections, flexible gop negotiators have
accepted  administration demands for billions of extra dollars. they
have  also added plenty of additional spending on their own.
''we're  trying to address some of the additional needs the white
house  has requested,'' senate majority leader trent lott, r-miss.,
said  of budget talks. but, he added, ''i'm hoping that the feeding
frenzy  will stop.''
contrast  that with the atmosphere five years ago. republicans
had  just captured majorities of the house and senate for the first
time  in four decades, led by gingrich, r-ga., their aggressive
speaker.
 back  then, with huge federal deficits envisioned perpetually
into  the future, republicans spoke of dramatically changing the
face  of government by eliminating the annual shortfalls by 2002 and
cutting  spending and taxes. rather than negotiate, they were
unhesitant  about using brinksmanship to force clinton into
unconditional  surrender.
''it  is possible the fundamental functions of the federal
government  could close,'' one gop leader, house budget committee
chairman  john kasich of ohio, said in july 1995. ''but that is an
opportunity,  not a risk. it gets us to the point where people
decide  where we're going to go.''
by  the following january, there had been two federal shutdowns
totaling  27 days. polls showed that an angry public blamed the gop
by  about 2-to-1 for the shutdowns; ever since, republicans have
taken  a softer tone on the budget.
in  gingrich's placed is the gentler-seeming dennis hastert of
illinois  as speaker. and beyond that, not only the rhetoric has
changed.
 by  the end of fiscal 1995, the last year for which a democratic
congress  wrote a budget, discretionary spending was $546 billion,
including  cuts republicans made by year's end. discretionary
spending  is the one-third of the federal budget that congress must
approve  each year, covering everything the government does except
for  automatic payments such as social security benefits.
republicans  lopped $9 billion off that total the following year
to  $535 billion. but beginning in 1997, the figure has headed
steadily  upward, practically soaring recently amid annual, year-end
talks  in which gop fear of another shutdown has given clinton most
of  the leverage.
according  to senate budget committee estimates, discretionary
spending  reached $617 billion in 2000, a 7.3 percent increase from
the  previous year. and this year's total is so far estimated at
$646  billion, a 4.7 percent increase -- with only two of the 13
annual  spending bills for 2001 enacted. that figure is expect to
rise  as the remaining bills take their final form.
''we  have a surplus now, we had a deficit then,'' said michele
davis,  spokeswoman for house majority leader dick armey, r-texas,
explaining  the spending turnaround.
republicans  say the extra spending is possible because of
today's  huge surpluses, which have so far gone mostly to reducing
the  $3.4 trillion publicly held national debt. in a radio address
last  weekend, lott said that still leaves money to boost ''our
national  priorities such as education, defense and health
research.''
 five  years ago, gop leaders were emphasizing eliminating entire
cabinet-level  departments, not increasing spending.
further  underscoring the party's turnaround, it was republican
senators  -- including lott and sen. frank murkowski, r-alaska -- who
delayed  completion of talks last week on a new land conservation
program  by demanding extra money for coastal states.
and  when bargainers shook hands on the new program and the
administration  hailed it as a triumph, it was murkowski who issued
a  written statement complaining that ''none of the accounts might
ever  be'' spent at all.
budget  negotiations are likely to last into mid-october, with
the  government kept functioning with a series of temporary spending
bills.

 editor's  note: alan fram covers the budget on capitol hill for
the  associated press.

[HEADLINE] :  look at govt. spending levels
[TEXT]
discretionary  spending is the portion of the federal budget --
now  at $1.8 trillion -- that congress must approve annually,
covering  everything except automatic payments such as medicare
benefits  and interest on the national debt.
fiscal  1995 was the last year spending decisions were made by a
congress  controlled by democrats. late in that fiscal year, after
republicans  took over congress, they enacted a $16 billion cut in
planned  spending.
fiscal  2001 began on sunday. fiscal 1995 through 1999 figures
are  from the congressional budget office; 2000 and 2001 estimates
are  from the senate budget committee.
1995:  $546 billion, 0.3 percent increase.
1996:  $535 billion, 2 percent decrease.
1997:  $549 billion, 2.6 percent increase.
1998:  $555 billion, 1.1 percent increase.
1999:  $575 billion, 3.6 percent increase.
2000:  $617 billion (estimate), 7.3 percent increase.
2001:  $646 billion (estimate), 4.7 percent increase, price tag
expected  to rise as final bills are negotiated.

[HEADLINE] :  more greek ferry victims found
[TEXT]
paros,  greece (ap) -- criticism of the government grew as divers
on  saturday found two more bodies inside a ferry that crashed into
rocky  islets and sank, raising the death toll to 76 in the nation's
worst  ferry disaster in 35 years.
marine  officials said the number of dead could rise further.
''doesn't  anyone have the decency to resign?'' asked
conservative  opposition leader costas caramanlis.
the  bodies were found inside the 345-foot express samina, which
sank  in rough seas two miles off the island's coast on sept. 26
after  crashing into the islets marked with a light beacon.
five  people were officially listed as missing -- four greeks and
one  albanian man, the merchant marine ministry said.
continued  confusion over the number and identity of passengers
on  the ferry has brought strong criticism of the government and
ship's  operator, minoan flying dolphins.
the  age of the fleet also has come under scrutiny, and the
government  has been criticized for what is seen as a feeble rescue
attempt  in which so many died even though the ferry sunk two miles
from  the shore. most of the criticism has come from the opposition
and  relatives of survivors.
the  ferry's captain, vassilis yannakis, his first officer and
two  other crew members will testify sunday before an investigating
magistrate  on the nearby island of syros, the region's
administrative  capital.
they  have been charged with murder, neglect of duty and
violation  of maritime procedures.
yannakis  has told investigators he was taking a nap and his
first  officer was off the bridge flirting with a passenger,
according  to testimony carried in the daily ta nea on friday.
six-member  diving teams were carrying out the search. measures
were  also being taken to prevent leakage of the 162 tons of fuel
oil  and diesel from the ship.
the  merchant marine ministry said 67 of the dead had been
identified.  they include a norwegian citizen and two canadians of
greek  descent.
the  european commission asked the greek government to provide
details  of the accident to determine whether eu safety regulations
were  violated.
''we  have asked greek authorities to provide answers within one
month,''  eu commission spokesman gilles gantelet said in brussels
on  saturday.
greek  merchant marine minister christos papoutsis, facing calls
from  critics to resign, said all passenger ferry companies would
have  to submit their safety records to the ministry by sunday or
have  vessels impounded.
more  than 10 million people travel on passenger and ferry boats
around  greece every year. there are about 18 ships over 29 years of
age  in greece's coastal fleet of about 130 ferries, many on routes
to  islands such as paros.

[HEADLINE] :  cops seek to block falun gong protest
[TEXT]
beijing  (ap) -- police checked vehicles entering china's capital
to  thwart a threatened protest by followers of the outlawed falun
gong  spiritual movement on sunday, the 51st anniversary of
communist  rule, a rights group reported.
spot  checks on major roads around beijing began friday and
continued  saturday after two weeks of police sweeps that saw 600
falun  gong members detained in nearby provinces, the hong
kong-based  information center of human rights and democracy
reported.
 the  report follows signs of government nervousness and
dispatches  in hong kong media that 10,000 sect followers were
heading  for beijing.
a  letter from ''all china's falun gong students'' recently
posted  on the group's u.s. web site warned of protests if police
stepped  up detentions ahead of the national day holiday.
china's  cabinet, the state council, issued a directive, carried
in  state-run newspapers saturday, ordering government offices to
tighten  security over the weeklong holiday.
the  order focused on general safety and demanded particular
attention  be paid to explosives.
although  falun gong members have kept their protests against the
communist  government's ban on the group peaceful, other disaffected
groups  have not.
turkic  muslims in china's central asian territory of xinjiang
have  waged a sporadic bombing campaign against chinese rule and
three  years ago bombed a bus in beijing.
national  day is china's most public holiday for celebrating the
communist  party's takeover on oct. 1, 1949.
chinese  president jiang zemin and other members of the communist
and  government elite gathered on the holiday's eve for a reception
in  the great hall of the people, next to tiananmen square.
premier  zhu rongji, in the traditional national day address,
made  only a vague, passing reference to worries of unrest by
unemployed  workers, poor farmers and falun gong members.
he  referred to ''difficulties of one kind or another in our way
ahead''  to a prosperous future.
people's  daily was more blunt. in an editorial to be published
sunday,  the party's flagship newspaper urged chinese to rally
around  the leadership to safeguard hard-won economic gains.
''stability  is the guarantor of reform and development,'' it
said.
 uniformed  and plainclothes police in the large numbers usual for
a  holiday kept careful watch on tiananmen.
chinese  by the thousands flooded into the broad square to admire
colorfully  lit fountains, floral displays and a 30-foot-tall
sculpture  of beijing's 2008 olympic bid logo.
chinese  leaders banned falun gong 14 months ago as a threat to
communist  rule and as a public menace that cheated followers and
caused  1,500 deaths. followers, however, maintain the group's
slow-motion  exercises, taoist and buddhist cosmology and the
teachings  of founder li hongzhi promote health and morality.
while  the hong kong-based rights group's report could not be
immediately  confirmed, the actions it describes fit previous police
sweeps  ahead of sensitive anniversaries. police have previously set
up  checkpoints and ordered provincial governments to round up falun
gong  members to keep them out of beijing.
another  group, new york-based human rights in china, said police
in  beijing have over the past few weeks evicted rural migrants,
street  children, beggars and others from the city as part of an
annual  clean-up campaign.
also  on saturday, french foreign ministry official jean
felix-paginon  said that china plans to revise, but not abolish its
greatly  abused system allowing police to send criminal suspects to
forced  labor camps without trial.
felix-paginon  led a european union delegation that met with
chinese  officials in beijing on friday as part of a twice-yearly
eu-china  human rights dialogue.
reform  of labor-camp detentions aside, china offered scant other
evidence  of progress on human rights in a one-day grilling, he
said.

[HEADLINE] :  florida man charged with killing 3-year-old connecticut boy
[TEXT]
	  hartford, conn. (ap) - a man who was granted temporary custody
of  a 3-year-old connecticut boy with cerebral palsy is accused of
killing  the boy at his florida home.
james  curtis, 25, was charged with first-degree felony murder
friday  in the death of alex charles boucher, who was on a visit to
the  curtis home.
on  sept. 25, curtis was watching the child and became angry when
the  boy soiled himself, authorities said.
police  said curtis scolded the boy, grabbing his cheeks hard
enough  to leave bruises, and later wrapped alex tightly in a blue
cotton  blanket and put him to bed unattended.
the  blanket was wrapped ''in a manner to willfully torture the
victim,''  according to the arrest warrant.
cpl.  jackie pehote of the police department in new port richey,
fla.,  said curtis admitted to wrapping the blanket so tightly
around  the child's body that it pinned his arms to his sides.
curtis  also said he placed the blanket just under alex's bottom lip
to  keep him from crying, authorities said.
curtis  returned to the bedroom about 30 minutes later to free
the  child because his wife, jennifer, was due home from the store,
pehote  said, but alex was unresponsive.
''he  had turned over on his stomach and choked himself,'' pehote
said.  vomit was coming from the boy's mouth.
alex,  who had cerebral palsy and other medical problems, was
placed  on life support at all children's hospital in st.
petersburg,  fla. and died wednesday. an autopsy found the cause of
death  to be homicidal asphyxia.
the  connecticut department of children and families, which had
assigned  temporary custody of alex to james and jennifer curtis for
the  september visit, had permanent custody of the child and had
been  trying to find him an adoptive home for most of his short
life.
 the  custodial rights of alex's parents had been terminated when
alex  was only a few months old because they were unable to care for
him,  dcf spokesman gary kleeblatt said.
dcf  initially placed alex with relatives in maine, thinking that
they  eventually would adopt him, but no one was able to adequately
care  for him because of his medical problems, kleeblatt said.
although  both maine and connecticut authorities attempted to
find  alex an adoptive home in those states, those efforts failed.
then  the curtises turned up.
the  young couple were former maine residents who had known
alex's  relatives and occasionally had baby-sat the child. since
moving  to florida, the couple had gone to a private child-placement
agency  in the hope of adopting. that's when they came to dcf's
attention.
 ''we  contacted that agency in florida and found that the family
had  already started the process,'' kleeblatt said. ''the agency
said  they had done a criminal record and child-protection check on
the  couple, and we received a letter from the agency saying this
would  be an appropriate placement.''
dcf  did not conduct its own background checks, kleeblatt said,
but  didn't simply turn alex over to the curtises, either. first the
couple  traveled to maine in early september and spent time with the
child  during two weeks of supervised visitation sessions.
child  protection workers from connecticut and maine thought
those  visits went well, kleeblatt said, and arrangements were made
for  alex to spend extended time in florida with the couple.
alex,  described by neighbors as a pudgy little boy with blond
hair  and a pug nose, had been living in the couple's apartment for
only  a week.
''we  are all very saddened by this,'' dcf deputy thomas gilman
said.  ''our preliminary investigation indicates that the (dcf)
worker  spent a considerable amount of time with the youngster.''
state  child advocate jeanne milstein, who was notified friday
about  the death, said her office will be investigating. milstein
said  she is going to recommend that the state child fatality review
panel,  of which she is chairwoman, also review the boy's death.

	  hackensack, n.j. (ap) - one of a trio of teen-age fossil hunters

michael  brandrowski, 55, of cliffside park, filed a lawsuit last
week  in superior court in hackensack against alfred siefker, 56, of
north  bergen.
brandrowski  claims that he discovered the 200 million-year-old
fossil,  is the rightful owner and that the money it fetched at
auction  belongs to him.
the  discovery of icarosaurus siefkeri in the former granton
quarry  in 1960 led scientists to revise the evolutionary timeline.
the  reptile - with a 10-inch wingspan and 7-inch-long body - proved
that  vertebrates were flying millions of years earlier than
previously  thought.
it  had been studied for more than 30 years at the american
museum  of natural history before siefker put it up for sale. the
california  businessman who bought it returned it there tuesday, and
it  will go on display next week.
the  lawsuit claims siefker ''wrongfully took possession'' of the
fossil  in 1989 and had no right to put it up for auction aug. 27.
siefker's  attorney, robert avery, said not only did the deadline
for  brandrowski to claim ownership of the fossil pass 30 years ago,
but  his client has documentation that proves brandrowski gave up
his  rights to it and was compensated.
siefker  has said he traded brandrowski a telescope, $30 and some
fossils  for the relic.

[HEADLINE] :  japan remembers nuclear accident
[TEXT]
tokyo,  japan (ap) -- anti-nuclear activists, some dressed in
funeral  black, called for an end to nuclear energy in a noisy
demonstration  saturday, a year after japan's worst nuclear accident
killed  two people and exposed hundreds to radiation.
more  than 100 demonstrators shouted through bullhorns outside
the  science and technology agency, demanding the government pull
the  plug on japan's nuclear power industry.
nuclear  power generates about a third of electricity in this
resource-poor  country.
the  protest commemorated the sept. 30, 1999, accident at the jco
co.  fuel-reprocessing plant in tokaimura, near tokyo, in which two
workers  tried to save time by mixing excessive amounts of uranium
in  buckets instead of using special mechanized tanks.
the  mix set off an uncontrolled nuclear reaction at the plant,
exposing  the two to fatal doses of radiation. a third worker was
hospitalized  in critical condition.
authorities  ordered 161 people evacuated from their homes, and
another  310,000 were advised to stay indoors for 18 hours as a
precaution.  in all, 439 people were exposed to radiation.
the  tokaimura calamity confirmed the worst fears of the
industry's  many critics, uncovering systematic disregard for safety
regulations  and inadequate employee training.
''the  government doesn't seem to have learned anything at all
from  the accident,'' said shin kobuchi, a 22-year-old university
student.  ''it has made no effort to listen to what the experts are
saying  about the dangers of nuclear energy.''
tokaimura,  a rural community 70 miles northeast of tokyo, is
home  to a nuclear reactor and a dozen related facilities.
the  town and plant operators commemorated the first anniversary
of  the accident with a morning of disaster-prevention drills.
this  time it was make-believe.
municipal  officials rushed to a command post after receiving a
report  of a dangerous radiation leak. sirens wailed. buses shuttled
evacuees  to safe areas, where they were examined by medical
personnel  with geiger counters.
last  year's tragedy continued to weigh heavily on a nation
suspicious  of nuclear energy and increasingly aware of its
destructive  potential.
utilities  are scaling down plans to build new plants. the
government  is struggling to balance its long-term goal of expanding
nuclear  energy with calls for tightening safety measures.
in  february, a state governor in western japan forced a utility
company  to tear up blueprints for a nuclear facility, citing safety
concerns  raised by tokaimura.
a  leading japanese newspaper on saturday criticized the
government's  subsequent efforts to strengthen its supervision as
halfhearted,  asserting the industry still lacks a vigilant
watchdog.
 ''safety  should not be left to chance,'' the daily asahi wrote
in  an editorial.
jco  was stripped of its license to operate the processing plant
in  march, and the last of the remaining uranium was being removed
from  the facility this week.
the  company also has agreed to pay out $117 million in
compensation  to settle 6,875 cases stemming from the accident.

broadcasting  corp. radio.
the  last three protestant ''loyalist'' prisoners were
transferred  to maghaberry prison west of belfast, and the lone
republican  prisoner was moved to magilligan jail in county
londonderry  on friday. seven other prisoners were moved on
thursday.
 the  good friday agreement provided for early releases for
prisoners  affiliated with groups which declared cease-fires,
including  the irish republican army on the irish catholic side, and
the  ulster defense association and ulster volunteer force on the
british  protestant side.
built  in 1976, the maze had eight h-shaped blocks designed for a
total  of 800 prisoners. the prison is southwest of belfast, near
the  protestant village of maze.
almost  from the day the maze opened, ira convicts refused to
wear  their new prison uniforms and smeared excrement on their cell
walls,  while on the outside the ira assassinated prison officers.
the  campaign reached its climax in 1981, when 10 prisoners starved
themselves  to death.
two  years later, 38 ira prisoners escaped, the biggest in
british  penal history.

buffalo.  a day earlier, the quinnipiac university polling institute

likely  voters.
while  clinton hitting the magic 50 percent mark for the first
time  this year in a major statewide independent poll dominated the
news  coverage, there was an ominous message in the poll for lazio -
the  mood of the upstate electorate. the poll found lazio supported
by  46 percent of upstate voters while clinton was backed by 44
percent,  a statistical tie given the poll's margin of error of plus
or  minus 3 percentage points.
''the  upstate stuff shouldn't be that close'' if lazio is to
win,  said maurice carroll, director of the polling institute.
buried  inside the poll was the evidence of lazio's upstate
problem.
 -thirty-eight  percent of upstate voters said they had a
favorable  opinion of clinton to 35 percent for lazio.
-when  asked if the candidates share their views on the issues,
58  percent of upstate voters said clinton did, the same number that
said  lazio did.
-asked  if the candidates had ''the stature to be a distinguished
united  states senator,'' 65 percent of upstate voters said clinton
did.  lazio hit 67 percent.
-asked  if the candidates were too liberal, too conservative or
just  about right, 53 percent of upstate voters said lazio was just
about  right while 47 percent of them said clinton was just about
right,  a very small edge for lazio.
-lazio  did have the edge, 74 percent to 45 percent, when it came
to  which candidate upstate voters felt had ''the right kind of
experience''  to be a senator from new york. he has made much of the
fact  that she only moved to new york in january.
-on  the issues, upstate voters gave the edge to clinton on
health  care, education and abortion. lazio had the edge on taxes.
-by  a 67 percent to 29 percent margin, upstate voters said a
candidate's  position on issues was more important than their
experience  or personal qualities.
that  last could be a major problem for lazio given that his
advertising  campaign has been geared to telling voters that they
can't  trust clinton. the poll found that upstate voters, like those
elsewhere  in the state, care more about where she and he stand on
the  issues.
the  upstate region, less of a sure thing for republican
candidates  running statewide than it used to be, has become
critical  in this year's senate race. in general elections, the
region  normally provides about 45 percent of the statewide vote.
asked  thursday in buffalo why he wasn't doing better in upstate
new  york, lazio said he thought he was doing just fine.
''i  don't pay attention to polls,'' he told reporters. ''if you
travel  with us and you see the crowds and the enthusiasm ... i
think  we're doing very well.''
asked  about the same thing at a later stop in rochester, lazio
said,  ''i don't believe for a moment that we are up by only two
points  in upstate. if you've been traveling around with me, as i
have  been going around upstate, people are exceedingly
supportive.''
 in  fact, clinton is generally greeted by larger crowds across
upstate  new york, certainly a reflection in part of her celebrity,
but  they also often seem more enthusiastic than those who come out
to  see lazio. she has been spending a good portion of her time in
upstate  new york.
to  counter that, the lazio campaign plans to have him spend more
time  in the upstate region in the last month of the campaign and to
spend  more money on television advertising there.
most  polls have shown clinton well ahead of lazio in heavily
democratic  new york city, which traditionally provides about 30
percent  of the statewide vote, with him having the edge in the
suburban  counties, which usually account for about a quarter of the
vote.
 that  has been the pattern for months, explaining why there is
the  heavy interest in upstate new york.
in  1998, then-republican sen. alfonse d'amato captured 55
percent  of the upstate vote to democrat charles schumer's 45
percent.  schumer won the election.
marc  humbert has covered new york state politics for the
associated  press for more than 20 years.
