[HEADLINE] :  handouts vs. works in brazil race
[TEXT]
jaborandi,  brazil (ap) -- mayor jose dias da silva thought his
record  would speak for itself. surely investments in schools and
hospitals  would sway voters more than beer or food baskets at
election  time, he reasoned.
the  problem was many residents seemed to prefer the handouts.
many  here complain da silva -- known by his nickname regente --
has  spent too much on public works and not enough on the favors and
gifts  that are a hallowed campaign tradition and give the election
a  carnival feel.
that's  where his rival, lidio dias, excels.
''regente  did stuff for the city, he didn't do anything for the
poor,''  says 19-year-old lesio villas boas da franca, who wore a
dias  t-shirt.
''unfortunately  the people around here are a bit backwards,''
explains  high school principal neusa campos de oliveira. ''they
like  a guy who gives them a slap on the back. lidio was the kind of
guy  who showed up at a bar and picked up the tab for everybody. a
lot  of people owe him.''
the  campaign ahead of sunday's municipal election in this town
of  10,000 people highlights the obstacles to rooting out the
patronage  and cronyism ingrained in brazilian politics.
dias,  an old-school politician and da silva's uncle, was mayor
until  1996. when his term ended, he used his popularity and
influence  to help elect his cool, businesslike nephew.
da  silva, who attended veterinary school and served in the army,
brought  something new to jaborandi, a poor town of mostly
subsistence  farmers 375 miles northeast of brasilia.
he  went after federal funds that dias had been unaware of or
ignored  and benefitted greatly from a 1998 law that distributed
government  funding directly to cities instead of through state
governors,  who often allocated the money at their whimsey.
under  da silva, the town's annual budget jumped from $47,000 to
$1.9  million. he invested heavily in health, education and a sewage
system  that keeps the local river clean -- something practically
unheard  of in brazil's poor northeast.
''governing  is the easiest thing in the world, you just have to
want  to,'' said the tall, stubble-chinned da silva.
dias  seems genuinely stunned by the transformation.
''my  administration was there to help the people who suffer on
the  farm,'' he said. ''i did what i could with my small budget.''
but  the all-in-the-family harmony broke down in 1997, when a
constitutional  amendment allowed mayors to run for reelection.
dias,  who had expected to inherit the mayor's office again,
suddenly  faced opposition from his nephew. the two no longer speak.
dias  says his nephew's administration wasn't entirely selfless.
he  points to da silva's enormous house and sports utility vehicle
and  suggests that he also benefited from the recent cash inflow.
dias  is known for distributing food baskets, a traditional
election  time handout here. but he claims da silva has upped the
ante,  buying votes with unicef funds intended to replace the
unhealthy  mud shacks that are home for many residents. instead of
new  houses, dias says, the mayor offers renovations, and only to
those  who agree to vote for him.
da  silva denies he's doing anything wrong with the unicef money
but  admits political realities have forced him to engage in some of
the  more traditional voting getting methods.
''i  do good work and i do good politics too,'' counters da
silva,  known popularly as regente. ''buying votes exists. if a poor
person  is sick what are you going to do: let them die?''
still,  da silva appears to be trailing among voters. his support
is  strongest among the 2,000 who live near the newly restored town
square,  where he has created government jobs and improved social
services.
 but  the 8,000 voters on the small farms surrounding the town
seem  to like dias better. he mixes easily with the farmers and
field  hands, drinking cachaca -- a homemade sugar cane liquor -- and
smoking  corn husk cigarettes. farmer jose joaquim saraiva, 56,
admits  the mayor has done a lot for jaborandi but says he still
plans  to vote for dias.
''lidio  helped me a lot when he was in office,'' he says. ''if i
could  pay with a liter of cachaca or a kilo of beans i would, but i
can't.  the only way i can pay him is with my vote.''

operation,  is an associate of the lucchese crime family. at a
newark  hearing, assistant essex county prosecutor judy gagliano
said  he has three gambling-related convictions, including one as
recent  as 1995.
arrested  in new york were queens residents flimer porro, 25, and
mercedes  odano, 54. authorities said the new york part of the
operation  was under the control of cuban organized crime figures
that  had allied with the lucchese family.

[HEADLINE] :  ap top news at 2 p.m. edt
[TEXT]
saturday,  sept. 30, 2000
gore  clings to electoral college lead
washington  (ap) -- the presidential campaign is tight and volatile across the
political  map, with al gore clinging to his lead in the hunt for electoral
votes,  according to an analysis by the associated press. sixteen states plus the
district  of columbia are leaning the vice president's way or solidly in his
column,  putting him at 226 electoral votes, 44 short of the 270 needed to win.
another  21 states with 175 electoral votes would go to george w. bush. that
leaves  13 tossup states with 137 electoral votes. polls show that gore and bush
hold  only narrow leads in several of their states, meaning the slightest
momentum  shift could reshape the race.
12  palestinians die in clashes
jerusalem  (ap) -- israeli troops battling several gunmen and thousands of
rock-throwing  palestinians opened fire on the rioters today, killing 12
palestinians  and wounding 523 in the bloodiest confrontations in the west bank
and  gaza strip since 1996. among those killed in the second day of deadly
clashes  was a 12-year-old boy who was caught in the cross fire and screamed in
panic  as shots hit a wall just inches above his head. the trigger for the
violence  was a dispute over a bitterly contested jerusalem shrine sacred to
muslims  and jews.
clinton  pushes school construction
washington  (ap) -- president clinton, bemoaning what he said are too many
overcrowded  and crumbling schools, pledged today not to budge from budget talks
until  republicans set aside billions for school construction and his education
priorities.  he also released a department of education study showing that at
least  60 percent of schools in the country need repairs and that the work would
cost  an estimated $127 billion. ''we're not going to leave the (negotiating)
table  until we invest in modernizing our schools,'' clinton said in his weekly
radio  address.
man  attacks ill. abortion clinic
rockford,  ill. (ap) -- a man drove his car into the city's only abortion
clinic  today, then damaged the facility with an ax before the clinic's owner
confronted  him with a shotgun, authorities said. the clinic was not open and
nobody  was injured. the clinic houses the office of dr. richard ragsdale.
ragsdale  filed a landmark 1983 lawsuit challenging illinois abortion
restrictions,  contending that they required doctors performing abortions to
conduct  their practices in buildings that in effect are hospitals. rockford is
located  about 85 miles northwest of chicago.
cops  seek to block falun gong protest
beijing  (ap) -- police are checking vehicles entering china's capital to
thwart  a threatened protest by followers of the outlawed falun gong spiritual
movement  tomorrow, the 51st anniversary of communist rule, a rights group
reports.  spot checks on major roads around beijing began yesterday and continued
today  after two weeks of police sweeps that saw 600 falun gong members detained
in  nearby provinces, the hong kong-based information center of human rights and
democracy  reported. the report follows signs of government nervousness and
dispatches  in hong kong media that 10,000 sect followers were heading for
beijing.
 last  inmates leave belfast prison
belfast,  northern ireland (ap) -- the last four inmates have left the maze
prison,  which is due to be shut down as part of northern ireland's peace
process.  ''it's an end of an era and i hope we never return to the same
circumstances  that we have had these last 30 years,'' prison officers'
association  spokesman finlay spratt said today. the good friday agreement
provided  for early releases for prisoners affiliated with groups which declared
cease-fires,  including the irish republican army on the irish catholic side, and
the  ulster defense association and ulster volunteer force on the british
protestant  side.
victim's  family plans ray lewis protest
akron,  ohio (ap) -- the family of a man killed after a super bowl party will
protest  outside the stadium before tomorrow's ravens-browns game. ravens
linebacker  ray lewis was originally charged with murder in the deaths of richard
lollar  and another man in atlanta in january. lewis ended his trial by pleading
guilty  to a misdemeanor charge of obstruction of justice. the pro bowl player
was  fined $250,000 by the nfl. a jury acquitted lewis' two co-defendants. no one
else  has been charged. relatives of lollar will stand outside cleveland browns
stadium  with signs and banners.

[HEADLINE] :  report: no charges in pa. shooting
[TEXT]
philadelphia  (ap) -- police officers will not be charged for
shooting  a carjacking suspect, but an investigation will continue
into  his televised beating, newspapers reported saturday.
police  fired nearly 50 rounds at thomas jones, 30, who was
chased  on foot and in cars after he was spotted driving a stolen
car  on july 12. jones eventually jumped into an empty police
cruiser  and drove off before being stopped by police.
a  television news helicopter videotaped police officers kicking
and  punching jones during the arrest, images that were broadcast
around  the nation. he was hospitalized for more than three weeks
with  five gunshot wounds to his arm and stomach.
the  city district attorney will not prosecute any officers on
criminal  charges in jones' shooting, saturday's editions of the
philadelphia  inquirer and philadelphia daily news reported. both
cited  unnamed sources within the police department.
officers  could still face criminal charges for the beating of
jones.  those who shot him could also face disciplinary sanctions
from  the department for violating its firearms policy.
jones  is being held on $1 million bail, awaiting a preliminary
hearing  nov. 2 on more than 70 criminal charges, including eight
purse  snatchings and two carjackings.
a  district attorney spokeswoman refused to comment on the police
statements,  saying the investigation into the entire jones matter
was  continuing.
the  shooting, chase and arrest is being investigated by
philadelphia  district attorney lynne m. abraham, the city police
department,  the fbi and the federal justice department's civil
rights  division.

[HEADLINE] :  runner ottey says she may not retire
[TEXT]
sydney,  australia (ap) -- don't say farewell to merlene ottey
just  yet.
in  what she had described beforehand as her final race, ottey
anchored  the jamaican team to a silver medal saturday night in the
400-meter  relay at the sydney olympics.
the  40-year-old has competed in six olympics during her 20-year
career  and won eight medals. but, within minutes of completing the
relay,  she was already hedging on retirement.
''i  said a year ago that after the olympics i would retire,''
she  said. ''but i'm not sure actually. i had such a rough year.
it's  been a fight trying to get on the team.
''i  had just two months to prepare for the olympics. i thought i
did  a fantastic job. i missed out on a medal in the 100. but i'm
happy  i came. it could have been worse. i will consider continuing
on.''
 ottey,  who finished fourth in the 100 last weekend, is the most
decorated  woman in track and field athlete, with 35 medals in major
championships.  but she never won an olympic gold.
ottey  said just competing in sydney was a triumph.
she  tested positive for traces of the steroid nandrolone in july
1999,  forcing her to pull out of the world championships in
seville,  spain.
the  international amateur athletic federation suspended her, but
she  was cleared by her national federation. the iaaf sent the case
to  its arbitration panel, which lifted the ban on july 3 on grounds
that  her urine sample was improperly tested.
ottey  was asked whether she felt any sympathy for c.j. hunter,
the  shot putter and husband of u.s. sprinter marion jones.
international  track officials said hunter tested positive four
times  this summer for nandrolone.
''i've  never really taken up for anybody regarding doping,''
ottey  said. ''i can only speak for myself. but if he's innocent, i
can  only imagine what he's going through, because i know what i
went  through. as far as if he did it or not, i have no idea. if
he's  innocent, it's a very, very bad feeling to be wrongly
accused.''
 the  gold in the relay went to the bahamas in 41.95 seconds,
followed  by jamaica in 42.13 and the united states in 42.20.
ottey  said she was thrilled that caribbean nations finished 1-2.
''i've  seen a lot of change in my career,'' she said. ''twenty
years  ago, when i started, we didn't have a lot of caribbean
athletes  on the podium. what's changed is the women are doing
better  than the men.
''people  are now believing in us. they believe we can also
perform  well and they're giving us a chance. in the past, we've
never  had a chance.
''people  should start paying attention to the caribbean. they
should  put more money into the region to support track and field.
hopefully,  we can do well in other sports, too, not just track in
field.''

[HEADLINE] :  lisa leslie happy with hoops gold
[TEXT]
sydney,  australia (ap) -- not even a scalping of lisa leslie
could  stop the u.s. women's basketball team.
when  australia's lauren jackson yanked off leslie's braided hair
extension  in a tussle under the boards, it was the final indignity
the  americans would suffer.
leslie  picked up her plait, tossed it to a startled
photographer,  then caught up with jackson at the other end of the
court.
 ''you  can have that,'' leslie told her. ''i'll take the gold.''
and  so leslie and the rest of the americans did, finishing off
the  australians 76-54 saturday to win their second straight olympic
title.
 this  was the kind of game the americans have been waiting to
play  since they arrived down under, an end-to-end rumble in which
they  unleashed two weeks of pent-up annoyance at the aussies and
let  loose a deep, talented lineup that seemed bottled up too long.
''it  definitely was our best performance,'' leslie said. ''we
had  to go out and make it an ugly game for australia.''
ever  since they came here, the americans kept hearing how they
couldn't  run with the australians, couldn't shoot with them, and
sure  couldn't beat them on their home court. the newspapers arrived
each  day with more and more taunts by the australians, more and
more  digs by local wags about how their beloved opals would give
the  americans, thrown together slapdash from wnba teams, their
comeuppance  in the final.
''australia  challenged us not just on the court but in our
hearts  and minds,'' leslie said. ''i don't think any of my
teammates  slept last night. we were tired before the game
started.''
 at  the heart of the hype was a real or imagined feud between the
6-foot-5  centers, the 28-year-old leslie and the 19-year-old
jackson,  stemming from a chest-bumping confrontation in an
exhibition  game before the olympics. leslie said it never happened,
jackson  said it most certainly did.
so  it came down to the gold medal game, and the trash talking
gave  way to some serious bumping and elbowing, players bouncing off
each  other and the court as if a hockey game had broken out.
the  packed crowd of 15,000 in the superdome added to the
feverish  mood, with green-and-yellow painted australians competing
with  flag-waving americans for supremacy. the american men's
basketball  team sat at courtside, lending its support, with alonzo
mourning  holding up a sign that read, ''usa came down under, got
gold!''
 leslie  and jackson did a fair share of the shoving, jostling
each  under the boards, going at each other from end to end. leslie
would  drive, and jackson would match her. jackson would pop from
the  outside, and leslie would try to do the same. jackson got in a
few  blocks, including a couple on leslie, and leslie came back with
a  few assists.
the  americans, with their solid bench and stronger, more
experienced  players, stayed ahead the whole game, building a 43-30
lead  at halftime and never letting the lead fall to less than seven
points  in the second half.
australia's  frustration mounted as the americans pulled away,
and  when yolanda griffith tapped in an offensive rebound to make it
70-51  with 5 minutes to go, the tension reached a peak.
in  a scuffle for a ball under the boards, jackson tore off
leslie's  long braid, leaving the embarrassed american to play the
rest  of the game with just a tiny bun on top of her head.
''she  pulled it off intentionally,'' leslie claimed. ''i was
surprised  she did that to me. trust me, she had to pull it really
hard,  because it was pinned in tight. she knew what she was
doing.''
 jackson  scoffed at that, saying ''my fingers got caught as i
went  for the ball. it certainly wasn't intentional.''
deliberate  or not, leslie's perception of what happened was
indicative  of how the teams felt about each other and how much was
at  stake.
''when  you feed us a diet of the stuff we were hearing, day
after  day, it's a lot of motivation,'' leslie said. ''i said this
day  would come, and we'd answer them on the court.''
the  americans answered with balance all over the court -- leslie
and  natalie williams scoring 15, sheryl swoopes 14, griffith 13,
nikki  mccray 8.
jackson,  who may be headed to the wnba in a few years, came up
with  20 points and 13 rebounds, but had little help from the rest
of  the opals.
though  annoyed by the hair-pulling incident, leslie said her
respect  for jackson as a player was undiminished. yet, leslie
couldn't  resist one parting verbal punch.
''i  have a gold medal, and i'm going home,'' leslie said. ''if
she  wants to come to the wnba, she can meet me on my turf.''

[HEADLINE] :  us hoops coach answers doubters
[TEXT]
sydney,  australia (ap) -- the final seconds were winding down and
nell  fortner was able to stop coaching and start hugging.
the  gold medal was assured for the u.s. women's olympic
basketball  team and the coach few had heard of three years ago had
answered  all the skeptics.
''i  wish you could jump inside my body and feel that personal
satisfaction.  it's just great,'' fortner said after the 76-54
victory  over australia ended her three-year run as the coach of the
women's  national team.
fortner,  41, was given the job in 1997 despite having just one
year  as a head coach on any level. she left purdue, a team to she
led  to a 17-11 record and the second round of the ncaa tournament
after  one season.
in  1996, the coach of the u.s. women's team was tara vanderveer,
a  coach with a national reputation. fortner was a well-known
assistant  at stephen f. austin and then louisiana tech.
the  players on a talent-laden u.s. were willing to give her a
chance.  she showed she could handle international competition by
coaching  10 teams in the three years to a combined record of 84-14
in  events like the pan am games, the world championships and the
jones  cup.
''the  biggest thing was that nell has grown with the game,''
u.s.  guard and soon-to-be temple head coach dawn staley said. ''she
had  one year of head coaching experience, none with pro athletes.
over  the years she had an understanding of how we approach the game
and  how she wants the game played and we kind of met in the middle.
''she  wanted things her way and she did, but we compromised in
some  areas and she listened. it was an awesome relationship.''
fortner,  who was an aide to vanderveer with the 1996 gold medal
team,  never looked at the lack of head coaching experience as a
detriment.
 ''i  always believed in these players and i think the key was
they  believed in me, too. that's a pretty good combination to start
with,''  she said. ''you work hard and have players who are making
sacrifices  and are committed to what you're doing and that makes
all  the difference. i feel very satisfied.''
those  final seconds saturday night, when fortner could stop
roaming  in front of the bench and start exchanging congratulations
with  her players, were the most special of the three years.
''it  was wonderful, a huge sense of relief, a huge sense of
pride,''  she said, her voice starting to break up just a bit. ''i
was  so happy for the players. they're always coming up behind me
and  saying 'nell, don't worry about it, don't worry about it.' but
coaches  worry. that's our job.
''there's  no question if you win with these players you're
supposed  to win and if you lose it's the coach's fault. that's a
tremendous  amount of pressure, especially playing on a contender's
home  court. i can't tell you how much i believe in these players
and  how good they are. it's been a great experience.''
fortner  seemed most emotional talking about five-time olympian
teresa  edwards and staley, her point guards.
''i  think a player-coach relationship is believing in each other
and  a coach has a different relationship with their point guards,''
she  said. ''with those two, they're the ones you've got to run it
through.''
 and  she did.
''i  think coaches, when they prepare for something as big as
this,  they second-guess themselves, you know, the type of team,
what  offense to run, things like that,'' staley said. ''but 't' and
i  had a talk with her just to reassure her that what she put in
will  work. you just have to let the machine run its course and she
listened  and let us do that.''

[HEADLINE] :  olympic roundup
[TEXT]
sydney,  australia (ap) -- michael johnson and marion jones
wrapped  up their olympics the same way they started them: in
spectacular  sprints to gold-medal finishes.
the  two american track icons shared the wealth on the medal
stands  saturday, both joined by their teammates in the 1,600-meter
relay.  and there was more gold for the united states: the u.s.
women's  basketball team successfully defended its olympic gold, and
the  men's 400-meter relay team finished first.
for  the u.s. track team, it started and finished with jones, who
thrived  in the sydney spotlight. she leaves australia with three
golds  and two bronzes -- falling short of her goal of five golds but
still  becoming the first woman to capture five medals in a single
olympics.
 she  also became the first woman since florence griffith joyner
in  1988 to win three track golds in one games. and she did most of
it  while shrugging off allegations that her husband, shot putter
c.j.  hunter, had used steroids.
jones  won the 100- and 200-meter sprints, took a bronze in the
long  jump and a bronze in the 400-meter relay, then closed out
saturday  with a gold in the 1,600 relay.
jones  ran a spectacular third leg, moving from second place into
a  comfortable 15-meter lead before handing off to anchor latasha
colander-richardson.  though not usually a 400-meter runner -- and
with  no desire to be one -- jones shared with individual 400
champion  cathy freeman the distinction of having the fastest leg of
any  runner in the relay final.
''in  a couple of days, when i look back, i'll probably feel
these  were successful games,'' jones said. ''i wanted to win them
all,  without a doubt, and i still think it's possible. but i
didn't,  so i'm not going to dwell on that.''
johnson,  in his farewell olympic appearance, anchored the men's
1,600  relay team to capture his fifth gold medal over three summer
games.  across an amazing career, johnson has won nothing but gold --
five  olympic and nine world championship medals.
he  earlier won the 400 in sydney.
led  by 100-meter champion maurice greene on the anchor leg, the
americans  won the men's 400-meter relay -- but the four-man team
drew  more attention with their-post race antics, an assortment of
wwf-style  poses and muscle-flexing.
in  the 1,500 meters, suzy favor hamilton's bid to become the
first  american medalist in the event ended when she collapsed about
75  meters from the finish line and finished last. suffering from
dehydration,  she was taken off the track in a wheelchair.
marla  runyan, the first legally blind athlete to compete in the
olympics,  took the lead early in the race because she was
frustrated  with the pace but wound up eighth -- ''which the first
time  around is not bad,'' she said.
''and,  i'm going to be back.''
with  one day left in the sydney games, the americans were poised
to  collect the most medals, leading the pack with 90 (38 gold, 22
silver,  30 bronze). running second was russia with 77 (28-24-25),
followed  by china's 59 (28-16-15).
------
 --  women's basketball: talk about winning on the road.
the  u.s. hoopsters faced host australia before a rabid crowd
eager  to see the gold medal come home -- and sent the locals home
disappointed.  the americans, winners of the atlanta games, took
their  second straight gold with a 76-54 thrashing of australia.
''we  played hard, we played great,'' u.s. center lisa leslie
said.  ''we knew we could do it, but it's still an amazing feeling
to  do it on someone else's homecourt.''
the  americans won with rebounding and defense, holding australia
to  31 percent shooting while winning the battle of the boards
48-27.  leslie and natalie williams led the americans with 15 points
each,  while yolanda griffith added 13 points and 12 rebounds.
the  u.s. women led by 13 at halftime, and quickly answered
australia's  one second-half run to put the game away. the u.s. team
has  now won the last two olympics and the 1998 world championships.
australia's  silver medal marked the nation's highest finish ever
in  women's basketball.
------

leipold  of germany at 167&amp;frac12; pounds.
u.s.  veteran sammie henson lost 4-3 to namig abdullayev of
azerbaijan  at 119 pounds.
gold  medalist rulon gardner, who ended the 13-year undefeated
streak  of russian super heavyweight alexander karelin, will carry
the  u.s. flag during the olympic closing ceremony sunday.
------

pool-length  away with 1.4 seconds left in extra time, was the game
winner  for the united states (3-4-0). the americans close out play
sunday  (saturday night edt).

[HEADLINE] :  no medal for diver mark ruiz
[TEXT]
sydney,  australia (ap) -- mark ruiz put on a smile for family and
friends  as he pulled himself from the water one last time. the
scoreboard  flashed 9s and 9.5s -- his best dive of the night.
but  that final flourish couldn't mask the disappointment of
leaving  sydney without a medal hanging from his neck.
''honestly,  i'm a little depressed,'' said ruiz, whose first
olympics  ended with a sixth-place finish saturday in the 10-meter
platform.  ''i'm not the kind of guy who's going to come in here and
say  no matter what i do, i'll be happy.''
the  21-year-old came down under carrying the weight of his
native  puerto rico, his adopted home in america and the mother who
gave  up everything so he could have a better life.
he  wanted a medal. he wanted it soooo bad.
instead,  he settled for a pair of seventh-place finishes in
3-meter  springboard and synchronized platform before placing well
behind  a 1-2 chinese rout in the last diving event of the games.
''i  shouldn't be upset,'' ruiz said. ''i did pretty well. but i
am  upset.''
tian  liang outlasted teammate hu jia in a battle of perfect 10s
to  win the platform, completing another stunning performance by the
chinese.
 they  became the first country to win five diving gold medals,
adding  five silvers just to drive home their complete dominance
over  the rest of the world.
china's  acrobatic army captured three of the four individual
events,  just as it had done at the last two olympics. the medal
count  reached record numbers because the program was expanded from
four  to eight events this year.
''they  were awesome,'' american coach jay lerew said. ''when
they're  on their game, they're unbelievable.''
still,  ruiz thought he could compete with the chinese.
a  16-time u.s. champion, he drew the obvious comparisons to greg
louganis  after becoming the first diver since the great one to
qualify  for both individual men's events at the olympics.
but  ruiz lost his edge when matched against the world's best.
''he's  young and inexperienced,'' lerew said. ''he needs more
experience.  he can do the dives as well as the rest of them. he
just  needs a little more time.''
growing  up in puerto rico, ruiz spent a lot of time at a river
near  his home, jumping from rocks and trees. his mother took him
for  swimming lessons, but he wouldn't stay in the pool. all he
wanted  to do was jump in.
a  career was born.
lydia  torres gave up her home and business to bring her youngest
son  to the unites states when he was only 13. they wound up in
orlando,  fla., sharing a one-bedroom apartment that was close to a
pool  where he could train.
''he  was born to be a diver,'' torres said.
ruiz  wanted to do well to repay his mother for all the
sacrifices  she made. he also felt the tug from both his adopted and
native  lands, fully aware that both were watching.
''i  let myself down and i let my country down,'' he said. ''i
wanted  to bring home this medal for the united states and puerto
rico.  i let them both down.''
he  wasn't alone. the americans captured only one medal -- laura
wilkinson's  surprising gold in the women's platform -- and left
sydney  with their lightest haul since 1912, excluding the boycotted
moscow  games.
david  pichler of butler, pa., who finished ninth in the
10-meter,  said a lack of financial support has left the americans
playing  catch-up in a sport they once dominated.
''one  thing they can do is take care of us a little bit,'' the
32-year-old  said. ''i have to work 30 hours a week just to dive.''
the  government-supported chinese showed their depth with the 1-2
finish  in platform. hu, only 17, was a late replacement for an
injured  teammate and wound up with silver.
''we're  very satisfied,'' said coach zhang ting, wearing a mao
badge  on his t-shirt. ''to get so many golds in an olympics is
quite  hard.''
the  chinese dominated from start to finish -- tian winning with
724.53  points, hu next at 713.55. defending champion dmitry sautin
of  russia earned bronze with 679.26, his fourth medal in sydney.
''every  time i see my chinese colleagues, i see a certain
consistency  to their performance,'' said sautin, a three-time
olympian.  ''i find it more and more difficult to compete with
them.''
 for  comparisons' sake, ruiz had 625.92 points after pulling off
his  best dive at the end. pichler totaled 616.17 to finish three
places  lower than he did on platform in atlanta four years ago.
''diving  is so deep around the world now,'' lerew said. ''every
year,  it gets deeper and deeper.''

[HEADLINE] :  gold-medal defeats turn ugly for us
[TEXT]
sydney,  australia (ap) -- a remarkably good olympics for the u.s.
freestyle  wrestlers turned ugly with two gold-medal losses.
sammie  henson, who said he wasn't interested in a silver medal,
got  exactly that by losing 5-4 at 119 pounds saturday to the
wrestler  he beat for the 1998 world championship.
then,  as he sobbed uncontrollably on the mat and during the
medal  ceremony, he was jeered by a crowd that felt he was an
ungracious  loser.
brandon  slay's complaint after losing 4-0 at 167&amp;frac12; pounds: he
wasn't  allowed to wrestle at all.
slay  also was jeered -- much more loudly than henson -- when he
wouldn't  shake the hand of referee borje schoug of sweden.
the  losses clearly upset not only the wrestlers but their
coaches,  who had seen the u.s. team win 12 consecutive matches in
sydney.  four more americans could win medals sunday.
''these  guys work six, seven, 10 years to get here and they take
a  lot of pride in winning gold medals,'' u.s. co-coach john smith
said.
 smith,  a two-time olympic champion, even wondered if there was
an  anti-american bias at work, intentional or not.
''the  united states was on good roll, it was obvious,'' he said.
henson,  of st. charles, mo., fell behind namig abdullayev of
azerbaijan  3-0 with 45 seconds gone and never recovered. henson
rallied  to tie it, but abdullayev scored the winning point with a
reversal  at 4:27 of the 6-minute match.
abdullayev  found a particularly useful way to fight off henson,
repeatedly  pulling on the straps of henson's singlet. the trick is
not  permitted by the rules, but referee abbass namazian of iran did
not  stop him.
''it  should be a point for grabbing the clothes -- he was
blocking  his moves -- but they didn't call it,'' smith said. ''we
needed  that (penalty) point, and we didn't get it.''
germany's  alexander leipold got his first three points on
penalties  against slay, with the other coming after slay began
taking  desperate chances to score.
''i  believe politics is a part of this sport, and it's
unfortunate  what happened,'' slay said. ''it's unfortunate when you
give  your all to win and it's taken out of your hands.''
the  match changed as slay and leipold locked into the clinch
that  starts the second period when there is no score. as they began
to  lock up, slay apparently did not position his shoulder the way
the  referee wanted.
''i  let him get locked and then i locked, and then he (leipold)
starts  whining and crying like a baby that he can't get his hands
locked,''  slay said. ''he was acting to the officials, hoping they
would  give him points, and they did.''
after  several seconds, the referee abruptly stopped, cautioned
slay  and called him for an illegal hold, giving leipold a 2-0 lead.
leipold  was later given another point when slay was cautioned
again,  apparently for grabbing leipold's hands as the german
applied  a gut wrench.
''what's  frustrating is i felt he couldn't beat me,'' slay said.
''but  i never got the chance to wrestle him.''
unlike  the early rounds of the olympics, when results can be
protested  and frequently are, there can be no protests of
medal-round  matches.
''sunday  is my last day on the job, and i can honestly say i'm
happy  and it's because of situations like this,'' said bruce
burnett,  who is leaving the u.s. freestyle team to coach at navy.
''i  saw a lot of points being put up and not a lot being scored.''
leipold  was elated to get a gold medal virtually nobody expected
him  to win. he has been unable to beat russia's four-time world
champion,  bouvaissa saitiev, but he was eliminated by slay in pool
competition.
 ''he  can come back and win two gold medals,'' leipold said,
referring  to slay, who turns 25 in two weeks.
slay  didn't especially like that comment, saying, ''who knows
where  i'll be in four years, or eight? i came here to win this gold
medal,  tonight.''

 russia  edged belarus for the gold medal saturday in the rhythmic

[HEADLINE] :  us quiets fans with win over aussies
[TEXT]
sydney,  australia (ap) -- there's been bad blood between these
teams  since atlanta.
the  australians had been laying for the americans ever since
losing  to them twice at the 1996 summer games and settling for the
bronze  medal.
this  time they vowed it would be different. their terms, their
turf,  their fans.
their  gold? no way.
capping  a perfect olympic tournament that was far more
challenging  than the romp through atlanta in 1996, the united
states  beat australia 76-54 saturday to win the gold medal.
the  americans did it with their usual combination of quickness,
depth  and strong inside play, knocking australia off its game early
and  taking the crowd out of it as well.
''we  wanted to stifle the crowd at the beginning,'' natalie
williams  said. ''we knew that if we got a good lead, they would
quiet  down.''
a  15-point lead in the first half did the trick and ruined what
the  australians had hoped would be their finest moment in
basketball.
 fans  roared from the moment their beloved opals first appeared.
they  waved flags and held signs. several australian athletes shed
their  shirts and painted their chests. it was like the cameron
crazies  from duke had gone down under.
not  that the u.s. players were surprised. fans had cheered for
their  opponents throughout the tournament and even booed the
americans  at times. when the united states beat south korea in the
semifinals  on friday, you would have thought the game was in seoul.
''they  were all cheering for korea and we knew there weren't
that  many koreans here,'' sheryl swoopes said. ''but we expected
that.  that's what makes it fun and exciting. that's what you work
for.''
 by  the end, the only cheers were coming from the small band of
u.s.  fans led by the players' families and dream teamers alonzo
mourning  and vince carter, who had draped himself with an american

 ''we  played hard, we played great,'' lisa leslie said. ''we knew

leslie  was too quick, williams and griffith too strong. williams

griffith  added 13 points and 12 rebounds. swoopes scored 14 points.
''i  felt if i got low and on the block, i could turn and
score,''  said williams, an explosive jumper with a wide body.
''they  were trying to double team, but a few times i got
one-on-ones  and i felt there was nothing they could do to stop me
inside.  i wanted it too much.''
the  game was loosely officiated and players often banged into
each  with nothing called. swoopes and australia's kristi harrower
jawed  briefly as they walked off the floor at halftime and the
crowd  booed when griffith and michele timms got tangled up near the
u.s.  basket and tumbled to the floor.
with  about 5 minutes left, leslie lost her braided hair
extension  and accused jackson of pulling it off.
''that's  all right. she can have the hair,'' leslie said. ''i
have  the gold.''
jackson  said it was an accident.
''my  fingers got caught as i went for the ball,'' she said. ''it
certainly  wasn't intentional.''
the  hair caper aside, this one belonged to the united states
from  the start.
swoopes,  who had left the south korea game with a sore knee,
scored  the team's first three baskets and fed nikki mccray for the
fourth.  everyone should be so sore.
the  united states led 26-18 after griffith and williams

with  five straight points to make it 35-23, and griffith's jumper
put  the americans up 43-28. that was the plan: jump on the
australians  early and don't let them up.
''we  had to,'' said teresa edwards, who collected her fourth
gold  medal in her fifth and final olympics. ''we were on their
floor.  we were on a foreign court. we had to go at them and hope
the  game went our way.''
australia  got the lead down to seven early in the second half
and  trailed 53-45 with 13:44 left, still plenty of time to make a
move.
 forget  it. williams had five points and an assist a 7-0 run that
took  the lead to 60-45 and it was never below 13 after that. the
united  states was pulling away at the end, and then the celebration
began.
 griffith  rushed into the stands to hug her 11-year-old daughter,
candace.  dawn staley grabbed the flag from carter and draped it
over  her shoulders as she paraded around the court, then handed it
off  and turned cartwheels.
leslie  also found a flag to wear and other players waved smaller
flags  on sticks, delisha milton strutting and gyrating as she held
hers.  they had come straight out of the wnba season and gone 8-0
over  a tough two weeks. a little dancing was in order.
''it  was definitely our best performance,'' leslie said. ''we
had  to go out and make it an ugly game for australia.''
it  was. but oh so beautiful for the united states.

[HEADLINE] :  pressure on boxers juarez, williams
[TEXT]
sydney,  australia (ap) -- for rocky juarez and ricardo williams
jr.,  a lot more than just an olympic gold medal is at stake when
they  enter the ring for sunday's boxing finals.
professional  riches await, but only if the medal is gold.
''the  only thing people remember is the gold. no one remembers
if  you win silver or bronze,'' u.s. coach tom mustin said.
the  $15,000 that u.s. olympic officials pay for a gold is nice,
but  for boxing gold medalists there's a real pot of gold in their
future.  oscar de la hoya found that out when he cashed in as the
only  american boxing gold medalist in 1992. four years later, david
reid  guaranteed himself millions when he knocked out a cuban to win
his  gold.
in  back-to-back gold medal fights sunday, juarez takes on bekzat
sattarkhanov  of kazakstan at 125 pounds and williams fights
mahamadkadyz  abdullaev of uzbekistan at 139 pounds.
a  handful of promoters and would-be managers will be in the
stands  watching to see if juarez and williams have what it takes to
sell  tickets as a pro.
''i  don't know if they (promoters) have been talking to them
already  or not,'' mustin said. ''i hope not, because it can be a
distraction.  but we can't watch these guys 24 hours a day.''
boxers  are prohibited from signing with promoters while still
fighting  for the u.s. team. but all bets are off the minute a gold
medal  goes around the neck of an american boxer.
de  la hoya parlayed his gold into a pro career that has seen him
earn  more than $100 million. reid got a $1 million signing bonus
after  winning his gold in atlanta, and has made millions more in
the  ring.
five  other americans won bronze medals in atlanta, and faced a
tougher  time making money. one of them, floyd mayweather jr., is
now  a champion and making good purses but even he had to start with
small  money fights because he didn't have a gold.
williams  may have the tougher road. he's fighting the 1999 world
champion  in abdullaev, who beat williams 11-4 when the two met in
the  second round of the world championships.
''this  is the fight i've been waiting for my whole life,''
williams  said.
juarez  hasn't lost in 68 fights over a period of two years, and
has  fought relentlessly throughout the olympics.
''this  game is just whoever wants it more,'' juarez said. ''i
can't  imagine anyone wanting it more than me.''
evander  holyfield, who knows a bit about fighting as a pro,
believes  juarez has a big future in the ring.
''he  fights like a pro now,'' holyfield said.
mustin  said it can be more difficult for u.s. boxers to keep
focused  on the olympics when many are already looking into the
future.
 u.s.  boxers will end up with only four medals in sydney, and it
will  be up to williams and juarez if the team will increase its
lone  gold medal counts from the previous olympics.
''for  other countries, their sole purpose is to win a medal for
their  country,'' mustin said. ''a lot of times our guys may look
past  the medal because they may have offers already.''

east  jerusalem, the west bank and gaza strip. he said arafat

israeli  soldiers against innocent worshippers in haram as-sharif.''
jordan's  most prominent opposition group, the muslim brotherhood
movement,  urged the government to ''cease all dealings with the
(israeli)  enemy and dismiss its ambassador in jordan as a step
toward  canceling the (1994 jordanian-israeli peace) treaty.''
in  lebanon, prime minister salim hoss praised the rioters in

trampled  the israeli flag.

banners  reading ''jerusalem is islamic,'' demonstrated on campuses
in  the mediterranean port city.

sydney,  australia (ap) -- hungary and russia hope their
gold-medal  match sunday is remembered for grace and talent, not the

the  olympic men's water polo final matches the two nations that
gave  the sport its most infamous moment at the melbourne games --
the  gashed face of hungary's ervin zador, pool water mixing with
his  blood.
''that  was another time,'' hungary's tamas kasas said saturday
after  his team defeated yugoslavia 8-7 to advance to its first

that  match came soon after the soviet union crushed hungary's
revolt.  zador, now living in california, says the game was a chance
for  his teammates to fight for their people.
the  hungarians, zador said, would frustrate the russians with
harsh  words and physical play. the goal was as much athletic -- to
win  a gold medal for hungary -- as political, zador said.
''we  got out of control,'' zador said by phone last month.
the  match was stopped with hungary ahead 4-0 as fans left the
stands  to surround railings near the pool. police escorted the
russian  team to safety.
but  the world was shocked by the pictures of zador's wounds.
water  polo, a sport known for its brutality, gained an even more
chilling  reputation.
''it  is hard for anyone who saw it to forget,'' zador said.
the  game's legend is strong in australia, whose people seem as
fascinated  with the contest as repelled, australian women's coach
istvan  gorgenyi said. ''they want to discuss it, all the time,'' he
says.

hard  feelings by the losers, russian coach alexander kabanov said.
the  battle is for both nations' return to water polo's elite.

difficulty  we face is the strength of the russian team.''
hungary  holds an olympic-record six water polo golds. but its
program  has faltered in international competition the past two
decades.  its last gold was in 1976, and its last medal was a bronze
four  years later.
hungary  rebounded from an earlier tournament loss to yugoslavia
in  its 8-7 semifinal victory. ''i think we beat the best team in
the  olympics,'' hungary's kasas said.
russia  was also a water polo superpower as the soviet union. it
won  gold in 1972 and 1980, then earned consecutive bronze medals in
1988  as the soviet union and in 1992 as the unified team.
the  crumbling soviet republic splintered its water polo talent,
a  pool kabanov is finally refilling.
the  russians ended spain's gold-medal defense with a thrilling
9-8  sudden-death victory saturday. the teams played nearly 18
minutes  of scoreless water polo until dmitri gorchkov scored on an
outside  shot with 9:55 gone.
gorchkov  says his only motivation is olympic success. ''we'll be
playing  the same tomorrow,'' he said. ''it's not possible to play
better.''

[HEADLINE] :  winners &amp; losers as e-zpass, toll hike debut on n.j. turnpike
[TEXT]
woodbridge,  n.j. (ap) - saturday was a day of winners and losers
on  the new jersey turnpike.
clay  daniel was a winner. out with his family on a gorgeous fall
morning  for a trip to new york, he was able to sail through the
toll  booths unobstructed, saving at least 20 minutes on his drive
because  he had an e-zpass tag attached to his windshield on the
first  day the turnpike began using the electronic toll collection
system.
 but  cornelius wade was one of the day's losers. an independent
long-haul  trucker, he found himself hit with a steep toll hike that
will  further eat into the meager earnings he says he's able to
scrape  out. saturday was also the first day of the turnpike's
so-called  ''variable pricing'' toll schedule, in which increases of
20  percent for cars and 13 percent for trucks took effect.
but  cars with e-zpass that avoid entering the turnpike during
the  peak hours of 7 to 9 a.m. and 4:30 to 6 p.m. won't have to pay
an  increase. during peak hours and weekends, the increase for these
vehicles  will be 8 percent. trucks with e-zpass will pay an 8
percent  increase regardless of when they use the toll road. it was
the  first phase of a multistage increase that will eventually raise
tolls  by 37 percent for cash customers, and 18 percent for e-zpass
users  by the year 2003.
needless  to say, there was confusion on the road as motorists
zigged  and zagged, trying to end up in the correct lane, trying to
figure  out how much they'd have to pay based on what time it was.
''it's  going to take some getting used to on the part of the
public,''  said lynn fleeger, a spokeswoman for the new jersey
turnpike  authority. ''for 50 years, there was only one way to pay
tolls,  and that was with cash. now there's different options.''
e-zpass  went online about 4 a.m. by 11 a.m., it had already
processed  36,000 transactions.
''it's  going very well,'' fleeger said. ''from a system
standpoint,  it's performing extremely well. there's still some
confusion  out there on the part of motorists, but we're pleased
with  the way the transactions are going.''
so  was daniel. the wilmington, del., man was out with his family
on  a trip to new york, loving the speed with which he sailed
through  the entrance tolls at exit 1 near the delaware memorial
bridge.
 ''i  love it,'' he said during a quick stopover at the grover
cleveland  rest area just north of exit 11 in woodbridge. ''i'm
thrilled  they started using it. you can go all the way from
delaware  to new york state now without having to dig for change or
take  out your wallet.''
''it  makes things so fast,'' said judy aldeghi of haddon
heights.  ''it probably saves you 5 or 10 minutes on each trip.''
but  those without e-zpass were less chipper saturday. lines of
30  or more vehicles queued up in front of some cash-only toll
booths,  while drivers seethed as they watched e-zpass-equipped cars
zip  right by them.
''at  exit 12 in carteret, it's terrible,'' said darlene deberry,
who  lives in carteret. ''it backs up halfway down the turnpike
sometimes.  i'm going to tell my son and my boyfriend to get
(e-zpass).  it might save you 20 minutes of waiting in traffic.''
and  the toll hike wasn't winning any plaudits, either. wade, the
independent  trucker from bridgeport, conn., was so angry he could
hardly  speak.
''jersey  is getting as bad as new york,'' he fumed. ''they
should  chop it off and let it drift off in the water. this is
another  nightmare, especially with what us truckers have to go
through.
 ''tolls  are too high to start with,'' he said. ''we're forced to
use  this road every day because we've been forced off the smaller
roads.  it makes it harder to make a living.''
wade  said he paid about $200 a week before the toll increase
took  effect, on top of $400 a week in fuel costs, plus maintenance
expenses  for his two $155,000 trucks. wade, who was hauling a load
of  steel from philadelphia to massachusetts, vowed to try to avoid
the  turnpike as much as possible.
''you  can't pass the increases on to your customers,'' he said.
''they  don't want to hear it. they don't care that it costs me 13
percent  more to drive this road now. they'll just say, 'take a
different  road; that's on you.' ''
another  trucker who would not give his name said the higher
tolls  would force him to break the law by carrying heavier loads
than  permitted, and lying about it on his documents.
''it's  going to make me become a bigger criminal, to load extra
weight  and run illegal,'' he said. ''it costs x amount to run this
truck.  if tolls go up, there's only one other place you can make up
for  it. i think lots of other trucks are going to do the same
thing,  too.''
the  turnpike will dedicate 120 lanes as permanent e-zpass lanes,
and  they'll stay in the same spots at toll plazas, fleeger said. an
additional  41 can be added or removed as demand dictates. e-zpass
lanes  are marked by purple overhead signs with flashing yellow
lights.
 fleeger  said there are no plans for now to add pavement markings
to  help motorists line up for cash or e-zpass lanes.
''we  want to see how our positioning works out first,'' she
said.

[HEADLINE] :  u.s. men clown during anthem
[TEXT]
sydney,  australia (ap) -- ''oh, say can you see'' is about to
become  ''did you see what they did?''
so  what should we make of the hugging, mugging, preening,
flexing,  posing, clowning display by maurice greene and his posse --
jon  drummond, bernard williams and brian lewis -- during the
national  anthem?
nothing.
 it  was a ''look-at-me thing,'' the kind of thing too many
athletes  do nowadays because they get paid and worshipped all out
of  proportion for doing one thing exceptionally.
over  the top?
undoubtedly.
 in  poor taste?
certainly.
 disrespectful?
 probably  not.
you  would know that if you had seen the look on their faces the
moment  it dawned on the four members of the gold medal-winning u.s.
men's  400-meter relay team how poorly their ''celebration'' was
going  to be received in the states.
that  moment occurred during a news conference a half-hour after
the  four collected their hardware, not long after a reporter read
back  comments by nanceen perry, a member of the u.s. women's
400-meter  relay team.
perry  watched her compatriots' celebration on a tv screen a few
minutes  after she'd stood on the same podium herself.
''the  whole way they were going about it, making all sorts of
comical  faces -- you could do a little bit of that, but they were
doing  it throughout the national anthem,'' perry said.
''how  do you expect anybody to respect our flag if you don't. i
think  foreigners think we're rude, anyway, so it just confirms the
whole  image they have of us.''
when  first read back to drummond, those remarks didn't quite
drain  the bravado out of his face, or even his posture. initially,
drummond  wasn't stunned enough to quit the annoying habit of
discussing  himself in the third person.
''jon  drummond never won an olympic gold before,'' drummond
said,  ''so sorry.''
drummond  was slouching when he said this, but then greene,
sitting  to his right, came across with an apology that sounded
sincere.
 ''i'm  sorry,'' greene said, his voice even, ''if we offended
anybody.''
 anybody  who knows greene knows how hard it must have been to get
those  few words out.
greene  is irritating on good days, so much of a peacock that his
vanity  plates back home read ''mo gold.'' he woofs and talks so
much  trash that even his pal and training partner, ato boldon of
trinidad,  often shakes his head at the crazy things that come out
of  greene's mouth.
but  boldon also knows, better than the rest of us, this is how
some  men pump themselves up to run 100 meters in less than 10
seconds  in front of 110,000 screaming people -- when years of hard
work  can be undone by one false step.
and  once drummond realized greene was on the level, he sat
straight  up and tried to explain how it felt to get it all right --
and  win.
''you  have a lot of emotion bottled up,'' drummond said. ''your
focus  is on one thing for so long. when we finally have that
opportunity  to exhale, we're just expressing ourselves the way we
know  how.''
williams  sat to greene's right and lewis to the right of him.
apparently,  greene's apology covered them, too.
good  thing that, because this is a fight these guys will never
win.  people back home will see them for a few moments on tv and
think  what michael johnson and a whole lot of their elders here
did.
 johnson  has an ugly feud already going with greene and he didn't
actually  see the celebration, probably because he was preparing for
his  own trip to the medals podium.
but  it didn't take long for accounts to get back to him. or for
johnson  to pass judgment.
''i'm  sure that what you all saw, that i didn't see, is a
product,  another product of that immaturity,'' he said.
that  was a contributing factor, no doubt. but this wasn't
roseanne  barr singing the national anthem before a baseball game in
san  diego, spitting and grabbing her crotch like a ballplayer. and
it  wasn't a political statement, a black-gloved, black power salute
by  john carlos and tommie smith on the podium in mexico city in
1968.
 this  was four guys letting off nervous energy. four guys who
should  have been able to find a less provocative way to do it. they
couldn't.  still, bad taste isn't a capital offense.
all  that needs to be said was said by antonio pettigrew, another
member  of the 1,600 relay team with johnson. asked what advice he
had  for lewis, the youngest member of the 400 crew, pettigrew
replied:
 ''what  i would say to bernard is, 'sometimes, don't get caught
up  in the moment. you represent the u.s.a. it's a bigger spectrum
than  here. it's all around the world.''

[HEADLINE] :  priest attacks ill. abortion clinic
[TEXT]
rockford,  ill. (ap) -- a catholic priest smashed his car into an
abortion  clinic saturday morning, then chopped at the building with
an  ax until the owner fired two shotgun blasts to stop him, police
said.
 the  clinic was not open and nobody was injured in the attack,
which  came just two days after federal approval of the abortion
pill  ru-486.
the  man drove through a door at the abortion access northern
illinois  women's center around 8:15 a.m. he was swinging an ax when
the  clinic's owner fired a 12-gauge shotgun twice. he did not hit
the  man.
the  rev. john earl, 32, was arrested and charged with burglary
and  felony criminal damage to property, said deputy police chief
dominic  iasparro. earl was being held in lieu of $10,000 bail.
iasparro  would not comment on statements earl made to police
about  a possible motive.
abortion  providers usually are on alert for violence following
abortion-related  events in the news, such as this week's approval
of  ru-486 by the u.s. food and drug administration, said vicki
saporta,  executive director of the national abortion federation.
''we  haven't received any specific threats, but anytime abortion
is  in the headlines we issue an alert to our clinics to take
precautions  because there is the potential for increased
violence,''  she said.
the  rockford clinic is not one of the naf's 360 member
facilities,  but ''it's just kind of common sense that clinics would
be  on alert,'' she said.
earl  did not reach the clinic offices with the ax, so damage was
confined  to the exterior overhead door he crashed through and
woodwork  in a hallway, iasparro said.
the  clinic houses the office of dr. richard ragsdale.
ragsdale  said there have been vocal protests outside the clinic
during  the past four or five months, but he said he had not noticed
earl.
 ''i'm  not surprised that something happened on the heels of the
ru-486  announcement,'' he said. ''but this is a little more violent
than  we were expecting.''
ragsdale  said the clinic will open as scheduled monday.
ragsdale  filed a landmark 1983 lawsuit challenging illinois
abortion  restrictions, contending that they required doctors
performing  abortions to conduct their practices in buildings that
in  effect are hospitals. under a settlement that was upheld by the
u.s.  supreme court in 1990, women fewer than 18 weeks pregnant may
undergo  abortions in clinics, while those beyond that term require
full-service  surgical facilities.
rockford  is located about 85 miles northwest of chicago.

[HEADLINE] :  russia researchers back from arctic
[TEXT]
moscow  (ap) -- russia's continental shelf may be bigger than
previously  believed, researchers said saturday upon return from the
country's  first major state-sponsored expedition to the arctic sea
since  the soviet era.
the  ship academic fyodorov returned saturday to the northern
port  of murmansk after a six-week journey, the itar-tass news
agency  reported.
vladimir  kryukov, director of the state-run company that led the
expedition,  said the results of research in the area of the
mendeleyev  rise ''could be a serious argument for expanding
russia's  continental shelf'' by more than 480,000 square miles,
pending  u.n. approval, itar-tass said.
kryukov  said the ship studied areas that previous researchers
had  not been able to reach, and that it was russia's first such
large-scale  expedition in nine years. the report did not explain
what  the researchers found.
russia  is already the world's largest country in land mass.
the  trip was sponsored by the defense ministry, the natural
resources  ministry and the state meteorological agency.

[TEXT]
monrovia,  liberia (ap) -- west african peacekeeping troops may
soon  be deployed along the borders of liberia, sierra leone and
guinea  to prevent war in the increasingly tense triangle, sierra
leone's  vice president said saturday.
albert  joseph denby said presidents of the 16-member economic
community  of west african states were ''working on'' stationing
peacekeepers  in the region to halt sporadic cross-border raids and
prevent  an escalation of hostilities. no final decision had been
taken  yet, he added.
denby  made the remarks to journalists in monrovia following
private  talks with liberian president charles taylor in gbarnga,
some  125 miles to the northeast.
relations  between the three nations have chilled in recent weeks
and  military activity along the borders have increased following a
crackdown  by guinea on hundreds of thousands of refugees who have
taken  shelter in that country from wars in liberia and sierra
leone.
 denby  did not reveal details of his discussions with taylor but
said  he delivered a special letter from sierra leone president
ahmed  tejan kabbah.
''nobody  is satisfied with events in the subregion,'' denby
said,  adding that taylor was ''happy to see me.''
guinea  accuses the refugees of being behind dissident attacks on
several  guinean villages while liberia counters that one of its own
rebel  groups uses rear bases in guinea to stage attacks on northern
liberia.
 the  economic community of west african states has sent
peacekeepers  to the past in both liberia and sierra leone, where
some  13,000 u.n. troops are stationed to salvage a lapsed peace
agreement  involving sierra leone's government and a notoriously
brutal  rebel group.

offenders  could be placed on parole.
in  coming years, 600 sex offenders sentenced from 1993 to 1998

parole,  department of corrections administrator donna thurlow said
friday.
 the  sept. 18 ruling came in the case of vance martin, who was
sentenced  to five years of probation after pleading guilty in 1994
to  sexual assault on a child. after two probation violations, he
was  re-sentenced in 1997 to four years in prison and five years of
mandatory  parole based on the 1993 law.
state  lawmakers said they would study the issue when the
legislature  convenes in january.

[HEADLINE] :  truth is elusive in airline death
[TEXT]
somewhere  behind the pale stone facade of the fbi building in
salt  lake city, the transcript from a cockpit voice recorder and a
thick  sheaf of interview notes hold clues to why a 19-year-old was
killed  by fellow airline passengers.
unless  jonathan burton's mother decides to sue, she may never
know  exactly what happened to her son aboard southwest airlines
flight  1763 from las vegas. he went berserk and was pummeled,
wrestled  into the aisle and held down so tight he couldn't breathe.
so  far, the more janet burton has learned, the more she has
questioned.
 little  about burton's last hour alive is sure. what follows is
the  best reconstruction possible to date, pieced together by the
associated  press from interviews with passengers, police, the fbi,
airline  officials, burton's mother and sections of the autopsy
report  she gave to reporters.
airport  police also supplied written statements from two
officers  who met the flight at salt lake city international just
after  11 p.m. on aug. 11.
accounts  differ, each influenced by vantage point, tainted by
media  reports or confused by panic.
------
 jonathan  burton was headed to salt lake to spend two weeks with
his  aunt and uncle. it was a trip he and his older brothers had
made  almost every summer since they were kids. during the week,
they'd  work at their uncle's painting business. water skiing and
hiking  ruled the weekends. in more recent years, the brothers often
went  separately, and this year, burton was traveling alone.
he  was getting ready to start community college after taking a
year  off. he had worked in housekeeping for a nursing home and last
year  was voted employee of the month, but wiping floors got old. he
still  wasn't sure what he wanted to do, but school would be a good
start.
 the  evening he was leaving, the tv news carried something about
a  plane crash. mrs. burton remembers saying it seemed like anytime
someone's  about to get on a plane, they hear about something going
wrong.
 ''yeah,''  her son said, ''isn't that something?''
they  headed for the airport and grabbed some tacos on the way.
mrs.  burton waited with her son at the gate until he boarded.
''i  love you,'' he said.
''see  you in two weeks,'' she replied.
carrying  a new book about how to win at blackjack, burton walked
onboard.
 ------
 adam  bradshaw jogged to the gate. his late flight from burbank,
calif.,  had almost cost him his las vegas connection to salt lake.
he  was headed for a new job and didn't want to miss the flight.
plus,  he didn't have a seat assignment on southwest, which does
things  first-come, first-serve.
management  consultant dave jennings, on his way home from
business  in los angeles, found a seat two rows in front of burton
and  tucked his laptop between his feet. dean harvey, on vacation
from  canada, settled in near the front.
bradshaw  found a spot toward the back. john whitaker, on his way
home,  was in an aisle seat nearby.
at  9:20 p.m., the boeing 737 lifted off over the sparkling
lights  of las vegas. it was behind schedule, but the captain said
he  could make it up. he told the 121 passengers they'd be in salt
lake  on time.
the  jet reached its cruising altitude, and the three flight
attendants  began serving drinks. that's when bradshaw first noticed
burton,  a young man in jeans and a t-shirt, wearing a baseball cap
backward.  he had brown hair, a goatee -- and a frantic look.
as  a flight attendant was about to serve bradshaw, burton
strolled  up, took a drink off the tray and returned to his seat
without  a word. ''away he went,'' bradshaw said, ''kind of
self-serve.''
 the  flight attendant told burton he should have waited for
someone  to take his order, but he didn't seem to pay attention.
a  few minutes later, he was on his feet again, this time headed
to  the rear. he rummaged through cabinets until he found the
peanuts,  grabbed a couple packs and returned again to his seat.
jennings  recalls thinking the guy must work for southwest.
another  passenger thought otherwise.
''fifty-one-50,''  the man behind bradshaw muttered. a television
news  director, bradshaw recognized the police catch phrase for
somebody  mentally unstable. bradshaw turned to his fellow passenger
and  said, ''i wonder what we're in for.''
several  minutes passed. suddenly burton got up and began pacing
the  aisle. he walked all the way to the front, all the way to the
back,  and then again toward the nose of the plane.
whitaker  thought he was looking for an open lavatory, but he
also  saw the glassy, agitated look on burton's face. he must really
have  to go, whitaker thought.
''when  he walked past me, he was mumbling and he seemed
anxious,''  whitaker said. ''he seemed stressed.''
then  burton raised his voice and kicked the folding door to the
cockpit,  hard. an emergency escape panel popped out and burton
shoved  his head and shoulders through the opening, muttering
something  like, ''fly this plane.'' people started to get scared.
six-feet-tall  and about 190 pounds, burton was muscular and
ruggedly  good-looking, bearing a slight resemblance in some family
photographs  to actor brad pitt. he had played baseball as a boy and
wrestled  at cheyenne high school. it showed.
it  ''looked like he was intent on going in there,'' said harvey,
the  canadian. ''but it looked like the pilots were intent on
pushing  him out.''
and  they did.
what  the heck is he going to do, harvey wondered. is he going to
pull  out a weapon? is he going to hijack the plane? what the hell
is  going on?
a  flight attendant urged burton to calm down. its unclear
whether  he then returned to a seat alone or if passengers forced
him  that way. in the end, a group of men surrounded him in an exit
row.
 somebody  yelled he was going for the handle. men and women
scrambled  over seats to get away. children started screaming. a
flight  attendant asked volunteers to stand in front of the broken
cockpit  door.
a  few men hung on to burton as he squirmed, then he seemed to
get  a hold of himself and sat down. the curious peeked up over seat
backs  to get a better look.
''settle  down, settle down, chill out,'' people told him.
''stupid  jerk,'' someone added.
burton  still had a sort of spacey look on his face, and each
time  one of his self-appointed guards glanced away, burton wriggled
a  little more.
it  wasn't over.
as  the plane began its descent into salt lake, burton exploded
again,  jumping up, spitting on people and landing punches like he
knew  how. kids were shrieking, old women hid their eyes. several
men  fought with burton. one wound up with a split lip. they grabbed
his  arms and legs, stretched him out and mashed him on the floor.
four  guys stood or sat on his limbs.
at  some point the pilot came on the intercom, telling passengers
they  were close to landing. he said he hoped all of them -- but one
--  would have a good weekend. its unclear whether this came before
or  after burton's second outburst.
jennings  couldn't see very well but remembers seeing burton on
the  floor, sort of under a seat. he says some passengers were
yelling  things like, ''hurt him! beat him up!''
''they  were scared. it felt like he was taking over the plane,''
jennings  explained.
only  he has publicly reported hearing shouts egging on a fight.
and  only harvey has given what may be the most disturbing part of
the  story. he says that in the end, it wasn't burton doing the
fighting.
 harvey  remembers the young man being subdued as some passengers
continued  to beat him.
''i  couldn't understanding why this was continuing,'' harvey
said.  ''he was basically 100 percent defenseless at this time. ...
i  remember thinking no one can take a pounding like this.''
harvey  says he went down the aisle as a big man in black boots --
weighing  as much as 260 pounds -- repeatedly kicked burton.
''i  felt this group, particularly this one man, was out of
control,''  harvey said. ''he was stomping on jonathan's chest, one
foot  at a time, i don't know what the rest of the group was doing
other  than holding him.''
harvey  begged for it to stop, but finally returned to his seat
and  told his companion, ''i think they're killing him back there.''
------
 one  of the first police officers to board the plane reported
finding  burton unconscious and bleeding from the mouth with a
''huge  knot'' and ''discoloration'' on his forehead.
the  time is imprecise, somewhere between 11 and 11:30 p.m.
at  first, fbi agent bill matthews thought he had gone into
cardiac  arrest. burton's behavior seemed consistent with drug use,
and  his heart could have stopped if he was doped up enough to act
crazy.
 it  would be weeks before his mother learned her son had
suffocated  as the other travelers restrained him.
that  night, police handcuffed the motionless body as people
warned  burton would fight if he came around. paramedics tended to
bloody  passengers and to burton, but he was declared dead just
after  midnight at a local hospital.
airport  police took passenger names, and some filled out written
forms  for southwest. others left without talking to authorities.
members  of the flight crew went to a local hotel and had to be
brought  back to the airport later that night for questioning by the
fbi.  the airline has declined to make their names public.
linda  rutherford, of southwest, said the company cooperated
fully  with authorities and believes crew members handled the
situation  as well as possible.
''the  flight crew did absolutely what they thought was necessary
in  response to mr. burton's behavior,'' she said.
autopsy  results, some which were released to various news
outlets  by burton's family, showed signs of drug use, but
authorities  don't think it was enough to cause the man's apparent
break  with reality.
the  autopsy also reportedly recorded that burton had contusions
and  abrasions on his torso, face and neck, and died after being
suffocated.  the death has been ruled a homicide, but u.s. attorney
paul  m. warner has decided not to file charges. warner issued a
statement  saying the passengers acted in self-defense. he declined
to  be interviewed by the associated press.
mrs.  burton says she can't understand any of it.
''that  wasn't jonathan. it's just nothing like jonathan. he
would  never go looking for a fight.''
now,  she's just hoping someone else will come forward to help
her  figure things out.
''i  can appreciate the fact of passengers being scared. if i'd
been  a passenger, i would have been scared,'' she said.
''but  jonathan needed to be safely restrained. he should have
been  in a salt lake city jail if he caused a problem on that plane.
they  had no right to be judge, jury and executioner.''

[HEADLINE] :  olympic notebook
[TEXT]
sydney,  australia (ap) -- it's become a sort of american olympic
ritual:  a trip to the gold medal stand followed by an appearance on
a  cereal box.
kellogg  co. announced sunday (saturday edt) that it will put the
faces  of four prominent u.s. gold medal winners on boxes of
breakfast  cereals.
sprinters  maurice greene and marion jones and backstroke swimmer
lenny  krayzelburg will join tony the tiger on boxes of frosted
flakes,  while swimmer jenny thompson will be featured on boxes of
crispix.
 all  four had signed contracts with kellogg before the olympics --
preempting  any plans general mills might have had to lure them over
to  the arch-rival wheaties camp.
kellogg  is an official supplier of the u.s. olympic team through
the  2004 summer games in athens.
------
 first  gold: the words had never been uttered before at an
olympics.  maybe that's why the players weren't sure how to react.
''gold  medalists: cameroon.''
the  crowd at the olympic stadium cheered, but the players on the
soccer  team hesitated and looked at each other. finally, they
realized  that this was the moment they were supposed to step
forward  onto the round, gold-colored podiums and accept their
medals  from international olympic committee president juan antonio
samaranch.
 cameroon  won the olympic soccer tournament saturday, beating
spain  on penalty kicks after a 2-2 tie. cameroon's olympic history
had  previously consisted of just two medals, both in boxing: a
silver  in mexico city in 1968 and a bronze in los angeles in 1984.
------
 at  long last: bahamians waving flags and cheering in drenching
rain  celebrated the country's first gold medal in 36 years and its
first  in track and field with the women's 400 relay victory
saturday.
 the  race ended around 4:30 a.m. bahamas time, but that did not
stop  people from cheering ''we won!'' in the streets. even police
officers  in squad cars honked their horns and waved flags, waking
some  people in nassau.
''i  didn't care,'' georgette hall, a 23-year-old mother, said of
the  early awakening. ''every bahamian should be proud of the girls.
they  are our girls. we love all of them.''
the  bahamian team of davis-thompson, sevetheda fynes, chandra
sturrup  and debbie ferguson finished with a time of 41.95.
the  bahamas won its first -- and until saturday, only -- gold
medal  in sailing in the 1964 tokyo olympics. when davis-thompson
took  the 200 meter silver this week, islanders were proud but
showed  little fanfare. the capture of the elusive gold brought fans
out  into the rainy streets of nassau.
''when  i heard the national anthem playing on tv i cried like a
baby,''  said marsha johnson, a 33-year old banker. ''it was great
moment.  i couldn't talk. i couldn't move. i am getting goosebumps
just  talking about it.''
------
 call  to the flags: olympics organizers on saturday urged
sydney  residents not to steal thousands of flags hanging in the
city  ahead of the paralympics.
more  than 10,000 of the colorful banners have been hung along
sydney  streets during the olympics and are due to stay until after
the  oct. 18-29 paralympics.
wheelchair  athlete louise sauvage said it would be a
disappointment  if the banners had been stolen before the
paralympics  began. ''i remember arriving in atlanta four years ago
after  the olympics ended and things had been pulled down and ripped
down,''  she said.
------
 derailed:  a train derailment caused minor disruption late
saturday  on services to olympic park, with commuters having to wait
an  extra 20 minutes for a ride to and from the main competition
sites.
 a  train went off the tracks at the flemington railyard in
western  sydney at 7 p.m (4 a.m. edt), according to the olympic
roads  and transport authority.
there  were no passengers on board and the train was quickly
removed  from the tracks.

saturday.
 the  wolverines (4-1 overall, 2-0 big ten) became the first
division  i-a football program to reach 800 wins.
the  badgers (3-2, 0-2) likely eliminated themselves from winning
a  third straight big ten title. since 1896, a big ten championship
has  been won by a team with two losses just four times.
henson,  who fumbled twice in the first quarter, was 15-of-27 for
257  yards and a touchdown.
on  the game-winning drive, henson converted on two third downs
with  a 21 and 20-yard passes to terrell. on the game-winning pass,
henson  eluded a rush, rolled right and threw across the field on
his  back foot, over two defenders and one of his receivers, to a
leaping  terrell in the back of the end zone.
henson  made his first college start after missing the first
three  games of the season because of a broken foot, and coming off
